==PAGE:5==
Preface
This report serves as the dissertation of a master thesis performed at the Department
of Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The overall
goal with the project is to identify physical instabilities that occur in vortex dynamics.
The tool for investigation is done by vortex particle simulations, which is a sub ﬁeld of
Computional Fluid Dynamics (CFD).
The report is split into 8 chapters; Chapter 1 is an introduction to the project where a
brief description of the overall problem statement and the method are given. Chapter 2
describes the theory regarding vortex dynamics, the mathematical initial setups, instabil-
ity theory and ﬂow diagnostics. Chapter 3 decribes the numerical method. Chapter 4 is
the ﬁrst chapter of results, that covers the numerical setup and accuracy of the structure
of a vortex ring. Chapter 5 is the second chapter of results, that covers the simulation
of a vortex roller. Chapter 6 is the last chapter of results, that investigates the physical
instability of a vortex ring. Chapter 7 is a discussion that critically relates to results and
contexts, as well as suggestions for further work. Chapter 8 states the overall conclusions
of the project.
Simulations of vortex rings and vortex rollers are performed with a hybrid particle-mesh
vortex method, pmlib, developed at DTU. The simulations are done using a parallel HPC
source. The Niﬂheim Linux supercomputer cluster of 11248 CPU cores with a total peak
performance of more than 225 teraFLOPS is used, and I send my thanks to the Depart-
ment of Physics at DTU and to Ole Holm Nielsen for his helpful support.
I also wish to thank Senior Researcher, N´estor Ramos Garc´ıa, for sharing his FORTRAN
subroutine with high order Finite Diﬀerence schemes, which in this project, have been
implemented in the existing pmlib.
Last, I want to send by biggest gratitude to my co-supervisor Henrik Juul Spietz and
my supervisor Jens Honor´e Walther. Their engagement, encouragement, patience and
availability, regardless time of day and night, have been highly appreciated and have been
a core part of the fulﬁlment of this project!
Niels Skak Bossen
DTU, Kgs. Lyngby, DK
22 nd of January, 2018

==PAGE:6==
iv
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Vortex rings and straight vortex rollers are simulated with a hybrid particle-mesh vortex
method. The aim is to identify the physical instabilities, that occur in which wavy vor-
tex modes occur, that eventually lead to the breakdown of the vortex structure. Vortex
rings are seen in real life, but is also a well prototype to turbulence, as convection, insta-
bility and breakdown of vortex cores are present. Investigating the instability improves
the knowledge of the transition to turbulence,s which is a highly complex and unsolved
problem. In this project the instability is to be compared to a Couette instability, in
which the ﬂow between rotating cylinders becomes unstable and produce visual vortex
modes which are also seen for vortex rings. The vortex core in this project is initialized
by a bump function, where the distibution of vorticity yields an initial total circulation of
zero, meaning that the vortex ring, in theory, will be non-propagating. This is seen to be
increasingly true, for increasing accuracy of the numerical method. The accuracy is also
governed by the vortex core radius, which converge towards a solution to the governing
Navier-Stokes equations for decreasing vortex core radii. The vortex instability forms
are present independently of the numerical setup. The investigations are based on two
Couette stability criterions, i.e. theories based on an inviscid and a viscous ﬂow. For
the inviscid ﬂow, Raleigh derived the critical criterion stating that the if the circulation
decreases radially, the ﬂow is unstable. For the viscous ﬂow, a critical wave number at
k ≈π is always present in Couette ﬂows at the onset to instability. From experiments,
the critical wave number is independent of the Reynolds number, and the simulations are
primarely run as inviscid. The analysis is primarely perfomed on straight vortex rollers,
due to their simple structure compared to the vortex ring.
Theoretical Raleigh criterions are derived for a bump- and a Gaussian initial setup, which
show that the bump setup is unstable while the Gaussian setup is stable. This is also seen
when simulating these vortex structures, both when investigating the temporal lifetime
and for varying vortex core radii.
This is also seen, when the vortex structures are
given wave perturbations of linear Kelvin waves, that develop into a spectral turbulence
stage for the bump vortex and vanishes for the Gaussian stage, which compares well
with instability theory.
The critical wave number does not match with theory.
For
simulations without wave perturbations, the critical wave number is sensitive to high
frequent wave numbers, dependent on the mesh resolution. If wave perturbations are
given, the critical wave number becomes equal to the highest perturbation wave number.
For further investigations, a LES model may change the sensitivity to high wave numbers.
Also, bump perturbation equations are derived, where the unsolved eigenvalue problem
to the solution form would be the critical wave number.

Resum´e
Hvirvelringe og rette hvirvelruller simuleres med en hybrid partikel-mesh hvirvelmetode.
M˚alet er at identiﬁcere de fysiske ustabiliteter, der opst˚ar, hvor bølgede hvirveltilstande
forekommer, hvilket i sidste ende fører til nedbrydning af hvirvelstrukturen. Hvirvelringe
ses i virkeligheden, men er ogs˚a en god prototype til turbulens, da konvektion, ustabilitet
og nedbrydning af hvirvelkerner er til stede. Undersøgelse af ustabiliteten forbedrer kend-
skabet til overgangen til turbulens, hvilket er et meget komplekst og uløst problem. I
dette projekt sammenlignes ustabiliteten med en Couette-ustabilitet, hvor strømningen
mellem roterende cylindre bliver ustabil og producerer visuelle hvirveltilstande, som ogs˚a
ses for hvirvelringene. Hvirvelkernen i dette projekt initialiseres af en bumpfunktion, hvor
fordelingen af vorticitet giver en indledende total cirkulation p˚a nul, hvilket betyder at
hvirvelringen, i teorien, vil være stillest˚aende. Dette ses, som mere og mere sandt, n˚ar
nøjagtigheden af den numeriske metode øges. Nøjagtigheden styres ogs˚a af hvirvelker-
nens radius, som konvergerer mod en løsning til de styrende Navier-Stokes ligninger ved
at reducere hvirvelkernestørrelserne. Hvirvelinstabilitetsformerne er til stede uafhængigt
af den numeriske opsætning. Undersøgelserne er baseret p˚a to Couette stabilitetskriterier,
dvs. teorier baseret p˚a en ikke-viskøs og viskøs strømning. For den ikke-viskøse strømning
aﬂedte Raleigh det kritiske kriterium, som udtrykker, at hvis cirkulationen falder radialt,
er strømningen ustabil. For den viskøse strømning er et kritisk bølgetal ved k ≈π altid
til stede i Couette-strømninger ved overgangen til ustabilitet. Fra eksperimenter er det
kritiske bølgetal uafhængigt af Reynoldstallet, og derfor kørers simuleringerne primært
som ikke-viskøse. Analysen udføres primært p˚a rette hvirvelruller p˚a grund af deres enkle
struktur i forhold til hvirvelringen.
Teoretiske Raleigh-kriterier er aﬂedt for en bump- og en gaussisk startopsætning, som
viser, at bumpopsætningen er ustabil, mens den gaussiske opsætning er stabil. Dette ses
ogs˚a, n˚ar man simulerer disse hvirvelstrukturer, b˚ade n˚ar man undersøger den tidsmæs-
sige levetid og for varierende hvirvelkernestørrelser. Dette ses ogs˚a, n˚ar hvirvelstruktur-
erne f˚ar bølgeforstyrrelser af lineære Kelvin-bølger, der udvikler sig til et spektral turbu-
lensstadie for bump-hvirvlen og forsvinder for den gaussiske opsætning, hvilket er i god
overensstemmelse med ustabilitetsteori. Det kritiske bølgetal stemmer ikke overens med
teorien. For simuleringer uden bølgeforstyrrelser er det kritiske bølgetal følsomt over for
høje bølgetal, afhængigt af netopløsningen. Hvis bølgeforstyrrelser gives, bliver det kri-
tiske bølgetal lig med det højeste forstyrrelsesbølgetal. For yderligere undersøgelser kan
en LES-model muligvis ændre følsomheden over for høje bølgeantal. Derudover er bump-
perturbationsligninger udledt, hvor det uløste egenværdiproblem til løsningsformen, ville
resultere i det kritiske bølgetal.

Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations

==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
The transition from the laminar to turbulent regime is one of the most complex and un-
solved problems in modern physics. At the transition, instability onsets become present,
where the initial stable ﬂows are subjected to small disturbances. For a stable ﬂow, the
disturbances will die out, where for an unstable ﬂow, these disturbances will grow to ﬁnite
amplitudes, which will become unstable to new disturbances, and eventually, the ﬂow be-
comes turbulent. Unstable ﬂows are characterised to critical thresholds for the governing
ﬂow case [9, 20]. As examples, the Kelvin-Helmholtz instability occurs in shear ﬂows be-
tween two ﬂuids, which form ocean wave-like patterns at the interface. This instability is
dependent on a critical Richardsen number, Ri. Other ﬂows, such as viscous ﬂows where
turbulent regimes become present, are dependent on the Reynolds number, Re.
Vortex rings are classiﬁed unbounded ﬂows, which are seen in real life as smoke rings,
mushroom clouds etc. These are torus/toroidal shaped vortex ﬁlaments with azimuthal
rotation around an imaginary circular axis, known as the vortex core [44]. The cross-
sectional ﬂow of vortex ring may thus by seen as counter rotating vortex pairs which
induce an axial velocity of the vortex ring, based on the circulation. Numerous authors
[3, 4, 27, 45] investigated the propagation and breakdown of vortex rings with Gaussian
distributed vortex cores. The propagation of a vortex ring is based on a non-zero total
circulation. In this project, a ”bump” distributed vortex core is used as initial condition,
which has a total circulation of zero. That means that the vortex ring is non-propagating.
Understanding the complex vortex dynamics of the vortex rings are important as the tran-
sition from the laminar to the turbulent stage covers convection of the centroid, diﬀusion
of the vortex core and, eventually, the breakdown due to instability [45]. The vortex ring
hence, serves as a prototype of turbulence. Therefore, identifying the cause of instability
for a vortex ring, improves the knowledge of the transition to turbulence [28].
Vortex rings are always unstable to the ﬁrst two critical wave modes induced on the
toroidal. For helical vortex ﬁlaments, three modes of instability are present; a very short-
wave mode, a long-wave mode unstable by a local induction and a mutural-inductance
mode [54, 55]. These modes have been possible to reproduce by direct numerical simula-
tions for ﬁnite Reynolds numbers [52]. Small disturbances on the vortex ring induced to
a critical wave number, form dipoles on the azimuthal. As they wrap around the vortex
core, strong streamwise toroidal wave modes are shedding hairpin vortices into the wake
of the vortex ring. New vortex structures eventually lead to turbulence, that destroys the
ring. Widnall [54, 55] investigated asymptotic theories with experiments and found good
agreement and similarities between vortex rings and helical vortex ﬁlaments.
1
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Multiple authors [1, 2, 16, 43, 50, 53] investigated the instability modes in a Couette sys-
tem, which is a ﬂow ﬁeld between rotating cylinders. Experiments have shown to produce
transverse wave modes, which are to be compared with the wavy modes seen on toroidal
of vortex rings and along the trailing edge vortices of aircraft wakes. Lord Raleigh inves-
tigated the inviscid ﬂow instability to be the unstable arrangement of angular momentum
between the interchange of ﬂuid, and is caused by a critical velocity. From the rate of
change of kinetic energy, the inviscid ﬂow is unstable when the circulation squared is de-
creasing radially. From this, the Raleigh stability criterion is deﬁned as the rate of radial
change in the circulation squared, which should be positive for a stable ﬂow.
Taylor further developed the viscous instability analysis by perturbating the governing
equations in a Couette system. This yields the governing perturbation equations with
a periodic solution form of the normal modes of the unknown coeﬃcients. Solving the
eigenvalue problem of these equations results in the critical wave number, which was found
by Taylor to be k ≈π with a corresponding wave length of twice the diﬀerence in radii
of the cylinders. This is commonly known as the Taylor-Couette instability [33]. The
aim of this project is to identify the instability of the vortex ring by comparing to the
Taylor-Couette instability. Due to the complexity of the vortex ring, qualitative simula-
tions on a vortex roller, which is a periodically extended circular vorticity patch, are used
for investigations. The analysis is based on the critical Raleigh criterion, the critical wave
number- and length, and by ﬂow visualisations.
The ﬂow is solved numerically using a hybrid particle-mesh vortex method, commonly
known as the Vortex-In-Cell algorithm, which allows for an explicit measure of the ma-
terial derivative of vorticity. The concept is that a Cartesian mesh is generated based on
the given spatial resolution of the computional domain [14, 15, 17]. The discrete vortex
particles are interpolated from the mesh and the particle position is calculated from the
velocity ﬁeld using a Runge-Kutta integration scheme [58]. The new particle position
is then interpolated to a new Cartesian mesh, which is called re-meshing. The Poisson
equation is then solved in spectral space to calculate the velocity ﬁeld from the vorticity
ﬁeld. The particle derivative of the vorticity, i.e. the discrete Lagrangian form of the
Navier-Stokes equation is solved on the mesh, before interpolating back to particles. The
cost scales as O(N log(N)), which is a signiﬁcant reduction for large number of particles,
N, compared to conventional vortex methods [13] scaling O(N 2).
As computional methods become increasingly eﬀective, this is a popular choise for inves-
tigations. Physical experiments are expensive and many ﬂows are diﬀucult to produce.
Additionally, the bump vortex is hypothetical and may not be possible to generate in
experiments. The computional methods have the limitations, that they need target data
for validation. Numerical simulations of vortex rings with non-zero circulation are often
compared to data from experiments [54, 56]. Another tool of investigation is a mathemat-
ical approach where the instability may be solved as a bifurcation, where the instability
may be analysed by diﬀerential equations as small changes to the systems, that cause
qualitative changes to the system [20]. As the bump vortex is hypothetical, this may be a
tool for validation. The bump vortex perturbation equations are hence derived, but not
solved, in this project. Gaussian distributed vortex rollers are used for comparison.
2
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:19==
2
Vorticity and Vortex
Dynamics
The material derivative form of the 3D incompressible Navier-Stokes equations is pre-
sented as the Lagrangian based vorticity equation, from the procedure of Kundu and
Cohen [33] and Wu, Ma and Zhou [61]. The Poisson equation, linking either the vector
potential or the velocity to the vorticity, and their Green’s function solutions, are pre-
sented. The governing theory regarding vortex rings and vortex ﬁlaments are described
in detail, and the initial conditions for the vorticity for varying ﬂowcases are derived from
a compact bump function. The theory of ﬂow instability, regarding to vortex dynam-
ics and centrifugal instability, such as the instabilities in Couette ﬂows is described, and
the criterions for investigation of the instabilities are presented in detail. Last, the ﬂow
diagnostics, for varifying the quality of the simulations are presented.
2.1
Vorticity-Velocity Navier-Stokes Equations

The vorticity is formulated as the curl of the velocity ﬁeld
ω ≡∇× u
(2.1.4)
and describes the local rotation in a continuum, where ω = (ωx, ωy, ωz).
Combining
Equation (2.1.1) and 2.1.4 yields the continuity equation of the vorticity as
∇· (∇× u) = 0
⇒
∇· ω = 0
(2.1.5)
where the vorticity is also divergence free, by deﬁnition, using vector identity.
Next

∇×
∂u
∂t + u · ∇u = g −1


(2.1.6)
As the gravity is a body force, working in the center of gravity, it does not produce any
rotation, therefore neither no change of vorticity. Hence the term vanishes. Due to vector
identity, ∇× (∇p) = 0, and therefore the pressure also vanishes. The new formulation
now becomes
∂ω
∂t + ∇× (ω × u) = ν∇2ω
(2.1.7)
where the right-hand-side is now only a diﬀusion problem. However, a decomposion of
the left-hand-side can be made as
∇× (ω × u) = (u · ∇) ω −(ω · ∇) u
(2.1.8)
and thus the Eulerian form of the vorticity-velocity momentum equations becomes
∂ω
∂t + (u · ∇) ω = (ω · ∇) u + ν∇2ω
(2.1.9)
where the term ∂ω
∂t is the Eulerican temporal change of vorticity, (u · ∇) ω is the nonlinear
advective term, (ω · ∇) u is the term of vorticity stretching and tilting, and ν∇2ω is
the diﬀusion term due to vorticity and viscosity.
Contrastly to the velocity-pressure
formulation, the vorticity stretching and tilting is a new term for the vorticity-velocity
formulation. However, the velocity-pressure formulation consists of 4 equations ((2.1.1)
and (2.1.2)) with 4 unknowns (u, v, w, p), where the vorticity-velocity form has removen
the pressure, and there is a kinematic relation between the velocity and the vorticity. The
particle form, i.e. the Lagrangian form of the vorticity-velocity form can thus be deﬁned
as a vorticity stretching and tilting, and a diﬀusion problem only as
Dω
Dt = (ω · ∇) u + ν∇2ω
(2.1.10)
where the advective term is removed. This equation is the starting point of 3D vortex
methods, as they are based on particles in a Lagrangian frame of reference. In chapter 3,
Equation (2.1.10) is discretized in time and space for the hybrid particle-mesh simulations.
The governing equations are non-dimensionalized in order to handle very small and big
numbers, which may lead to numerical troubles. The continuity equation then becomes

2.2
The Poisson Equation
No continous analytical solution exists to the Navier-Stokes equations. The aim is to
determine approximate values of the velocity and/or the vorticity. For that, the velocity
is deﬁned from the solenoidal vector potential , ψ, and the scalar potential, φ, as
u ≡∇× ψ + ∇φ
(2.2.1)
where the vector potential by the deﬁnition
∇· ψ = 0
(2.2.2)
ensures a divergence free velocity ﬁeld. If Equation (2.2.1) is substituted into Equation
(2.1.4), this yields a Poisson equation
∇2ψ = −ω
(2.2.3)
which relates the vector potential to the vorticity. A Poisson equation relating the velocity
to the vorticity may also be derived by taking the curl of Equation (2.1.4).
∇2u = −∇× ω
(2.2.4)
In an unbounded domain R, the Poisson equations are solved using convolutions of Green’s
functions (See Chapter 3).

2.3
Circulation and Vortex Theorems
The circulation of the vorticity ﬁeld is deﬁned as
Γ =
I
C
u · dS =
Z
A
ω · ndA
(2.3.1)
where C is a closed curve, A is a surface bounded area and n is the normal vector. It
states that the circulation is the line integral of the velocity ﬁeld around a closed curve.
This may, through Stokes theorem be expressed as the ﬂux through a surface A closed by
C. A vortex line is a curve of ﬂuid, which is always tangent to the local vorticity vector.
A vortex line does not have to be linear, as it may be curved. In analogy, a vortex line is
related to the vorticity ﬁeld, corresponding to a streamline is related to the velocity vector.
A region of vortex lines through a closed curve, is called a vortex tube. This cylinder
shaped form is, again, related to a stream tube of streamlines through a closed surface.
The strength of a streamtube is deﬁned as the ﬂux, Q, through the surface, calculated as
an areal integral of the velocity through the the area. The strength of a vortex tube is
deﬁned as the circulation, Γ, which may be computed from Equation (2.3.1). The analogy
between the streamtube and the vortex tube may be seen in Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Analogy between a streamtube and a vortex tube. Lines are tangent to velocity
ﬁelds and vorticity ﬁelds, respectively. The strengths are the ﬂux, Q, and the circulation, Γ,
respectively [33].
Last, a vortex ﬁlament or a vortex sheet is where there is a discontinuity in the ﬂuid
region. This may be seen as if the sorrounding ﬂuid of the vortex tube is irrotational.
Kelvin’s circulation theorem states that the circulation around a closed curve moving
with the ﬂow, remains constant in time, in a non-rotating frame of reference, if the ﬂow
is inviscid and barotropic with conservative body forces. This may be expressed as
DΓ
Dt = 0
(2.3.2)
6
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
Due to this statement, this theorem may only be violated if viscous diﬀusion applies or
if the ﬂuid is to interact with a solid boundary. The vortex ring is simulated as an un-
bounded ﬂow, so therefore no solid boundary is present in this project. Viscous diﬀusion
is only present if viscosity eﬀects are included in the simulation. However, due to numer-
ical diﬀusion (see later in this chapter), Kelvin’s theorem may be violated. Gravity and
pressure are neglected since the governing momentum equations have been deﬁned on the
Lagrangian form.
From Kelvin’s vortex theorem, the Helmholtz’s vortex theorems are deﬁned for vortex
motion as
1. Vortex lines move with the ﬂuid
2. The strength of a vortex tube (its circulation) is constant along its length
3. A vortex tube cannot end within the ﬂuid. It must either
(a) end at a solid boundary, or
(b) form a closed loop - a vortex ring, or
(c) extend to ±∞
4. The strength of a vortex tube remains constant in time
The theorems state that ﬂuid particles that at any time are coherent with a vortex line,
always remain coherent to that same vortex line.
2.4
Vortex Rings
Vortex rings are torus/toroidal shaped vortex ﬁlaments, where the ﬂuid mostly circulate
an imaginary circular axis, known as the vortex core. Two rotating frames are present
for the torus shape, where the primary motion is poloidal, and the secondary is toroidal.
The reference frames may be seen in Figure 2.2.
Figure 2.2: Rotating frames of the vortex ring, showing the dominant azimuthal ﬂow as the
poloidal, ϕ, and the normal to azimuthal frame as toroidal, θ.
The circulation around the vortex core induces a velocity which translates perpendicular
to the plane of the vortex ring. The inner edge of the vortex core moves faster than the
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
7
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
outer edge of the core, meaning that the velocity is almost constant at the outer edge
of the core. The inner core has increased angular velocity due to higher concentration
of vorticity. Therefore, most of the energy dissipation, will happen in this region, due
to increased enstrophy, which will be presented later in this section. Within the vortex
core, the vorticity may be approximated as a vortex line, with a strength equal to the
total circulation. Further, it is assumed that the sorrounding volume of ﬂuid is ideal, and
hence irrotational. Thus the cross-sectional velocity may be simpliﬁed as the interaction
of two counter rotating vortices of equal magnitude of vorticity as
U ≈
Γ
4πR
(2.4.1)
where R is the vortex ring radius and Γ may be computed using Equation (2.3.1). The
cross-section of the vortex ring and the induced velocity from counter rotating vortices
may be seen in Figure 2.3.
(a)

Figure 2.3: (a) Vortex ring dimensions in the xr-plane, sketching the vortex ring radius, R,

(b) Induced velocity, perpendicular to ring radius, from the interaction of counter rotating vor-
tices of equal magnitude of circulation
The vortex ring is initially axisymmetric in the yz-plane, which is a circular cross sec-
tion. The vortex ring propagates in the positive x-direction. The vortex ring radius is
measured from the centroid of the ring to the center of the vortex core. The vortex core ra-
dius, σ, is measured from the center of the vortex core to the outer edge of the vortex core.

Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
Vortex rings are seen in nature for turbulent ﬂows, but are rarely noticed on their own.
Examples of the physical visualization of vortex rings may be seen from smoke rings,
mushroom clouds and microbursts. They are also seen as more complex knotted vortices
in plasmas, which is investigated by Kleckner and Irvine [30].
Due to their existance for turbulent ﬂows, they are suitable prototypes of particle based
simulations of turbulence as their development from the laminar to a turbulent regime
cover ﬁelds as convection, diﬀusion and ﬂow instability, which eventually lead to their
breakdown [3, 44]. The instability of the vortex ring may be seen on the transition from
the laminar to the turbulent stage. The physical instability of the ring may be seen as
azimuthal wave perturbations on the outer edge of the vortex core, which by time will
transfer to the the vicinity of the inner edge of the vortex core. Archer [3] discovered
these instabilities already in the laminar regime. Vortex rings with Gaussian distributed
vortex cores are studied by numerous authors, etc. [3, 4], where the ring structure tends
to be skewed in the transition as well as the vortex core goes from an initial Gaussian
state to a non-Gaussian state that causes shedding of vortex ﬁlaments in the wake of the
vortex ring [45].
2.4.1
Compact Vortex Bump Function
From the Poisson equation (2.2.3), it follows that the vorticity is solenoidal and compact
only if the vector potential is [40]. The compact state of the vortex ring is saught af-
ter, to ensure minimum truncation and ensure conservation of the vorticity in the vortex
core. Therefore the mathematical distribution of the vector potential is deﬁned in order
to distribute the corresponding vorticity ﬁeld. As mentioned, numerous authors used the
Gaussian distribution to describe the vorticity distribution. However, that entails a singu-
larity in the origin of the corresponding vorticity ﬁeld, which leads to a decay of vorticity,
that entails discontinuity of the diﬀerential operator. Hence the vector potential has a
ﬁnite number of derivatives, which weakness the convergence [40].
Instead the mathematical formulation of the vector potential is governed by a ”bump”
function, which is a smooth function in Euclidean space Rn. The smoothness is deﬁned as
it has inﬁnite number of derivatives, C∞, is ﬁnite valued and has compact support of the
vortex core [25, 26, 40]. The mathematical formulation of the bump function is deﬁned
as
Φ(τ) =
(
exp

−
c
1−τ2

for |τ|< 1
0
for |τ|≥1
(2.4.2)
where τ is the support domain of the smooth vector potential and c is an arbitrary bump
constant. This parameter, c, governs the vortex core radius, as the vortex core descreases
for increasing c. Also it has been seen that for a c = 400, minimum amount of laminar
sheddings of vortex ﬁlaments were spotted from the transition between the laminar and
the tubulent regime [6].
To generate the bump vortex ring, the mathematical distribution of the torus ring must
be deﬁned, where the torus core radius distribution may be inserted in τ.

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
The aim is ﬁrst to use the mathemtical formulation of the torus ring as the input function
of the bump function. Later, that torus ring will be ”unfolded” to generate a cylinder
shaped vortex roller. This is done to investigate if the same instabilities occur for both
vortex structures. Referring to the Helmholtz’s theorems the vortex tube must be closed,
which is easily seen for the vortex ring as it forms a closed loop. The boundary conditions
in the z-direction of the vortex roller must therefore extend to ±∞, which is achieved by
imposing semi periodic-unbounded conditions, which is described in the Chapter 3. The
analogy between the vortex ring and the vortex roller is seen in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Analogy between the vortex ring and the vortex roller in Cartesian coordinates. The
vortex ring and the vortex roller have propagational x-direction. The vortex roller is periodic in
the z-direction to fulﬁl the Helmholtz vortex theorem
The vector potentials are to be generated for the structures, and by the use of the Pois-
son equation the 3D vorticity ﬁelds and the corresponding velocity ﬁelds for the initial
conditions are deﬁned.

2.4.1.2
3D Scalar Distribution: Semi-Periodic Vortex Roller

2.4.3
Perturbating the Centerline for Semi-Periodic Domains
For the case of a 2D vorticity patch, extended to a pseudo 3D vortex roller, the nonlinear
term of the Navier-Stokes equations vanishes. Further, the diﬀusion term is zero if the
ﬂow is inviscid.
Thus, a perturbation may be necessary to onset an instability.
The
perturbations are added by a ﬁnite number of initial waves extended in the z-direction.
The length scale of the perturbation, Lz, is governed by the computational domain in the
periodic direction, and random phases, ςi. The perturbation is then given as

2.5
Flow Instability
Flow phenomena, that completely satisfy the conservation laws, are seldom observed in
reality, as these become unstable when subjected to small disturbances, which are invari-
ably present in any ﬂow [33]. In viscous ﬂows, the ﬂow is only stable to small disturbances,
when certain conditions are satisﬁed. That being the Reynolds number to be below a cer-
tain limit or the Richardsen number, Ri, in shear ﬂows, to be above a certain limit etc.
As a consequence of the critical stability limits are avoided, inﬁnitesimal disturbances
may grow in the ﬂow ﬁeld and change the initial state of the ﬂow to an entirely new and
increasingly complex ﬂow. That being, that the disturbances grow to ﬁnite amplitudes,
and the new state may also become unstable to new disturbances, which also induce new
instability modes. Maxwell deﬁned the stable and unstable nature of ﬂows as:
”When the state of things is such that an inﬁnitely small variation of the
present state will alter only by an inﬁnitely small quantity the state at some
future time, the condition of the system, whether at rest or in motion, is
said to be stable; but when an inﬁnitely small variation in the present state
may bring about a ﬁnite diﬀerence in the state of the system in a ﬁnite
time, the condition of the system is said to be unstable [20].”
As the unstable ﬂows evolve, these ﬂows are characterized as the superposition of all
interacting disturbances. This produces nearly random phases and a highly chaotic state,
that induces a turbulent ﬂow ﬁeld. Therefore, investigating the ﬂow instabilities gives
better understanding of
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
17
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
• The transition from the laminar to turbulent regimes
• Predicting the onset to the transition
When analysing the instability onset to ﬂuid ﬂows, the disturbances in the ﬂow is intro-
duced by small perturbations which are small enough, such that linearization is possible
when neglecting high order pertubation terms. That also means, that the initial state,
known as the background state, is not subjected to visuable disturbances. For an unstable
background state ﬂow, the perturbations introduced, will tend to grow and produce sec-
ondary and higher order ﬂows [9]. As a consequence, at least one mode of disturbance is
present, which distorts the ﬂow from the background state [10]. Opposite, the perturba-
tions will decay and vanish for a stable ﬂow [9]. However, linearization may only predict
the onset to instability, but not necessary the transition to turbulence. The linearization
is however very well documented for the Taylor-Couette ﬂow instability within rotating
cylinders, in which the secondary wavy ﬂows are induced, and conﬁrmed by experiments
by multiple authors. The criterions of this instability are investigated later in this section.
Mathematically, the instability may be investigated based on the method of normal modes
[33], in which the instability is considered as a wave mode along an axis in the ﬂow
ω(x, y, z, t) = bω(z) exp (ιkx + ιmy + ςt) = bω(z) exp (ι|K|(eK · x −cςt))
(2.5.1)
where b() denotes a Fourier coeﬃcient, ι = √−1 is the imaginary unit, K = (k, m, 0) is the
distubance wave number, ς is the temporal growth rate, cς is the complex phase speed of
disturbances and eK = K/|K|. The Fourier componets of such a stationary and travelling
wave form is known as the normal mode, and is exponentially varying in space and time,
based on the spatial dimension and a temporal growth rate. If two directions of the ﬂow
ﬁeld are unbounded [37], the wave number components in the bounded direction, must
be real and positive. An arbitrary disturbance may be decomposed into a set of normal
modes, where the linearization of the problem implies, that the modes do not interact.
Therefore the critical wave numbers are found as the eigenvalue problem of the solution
form to the perturbations in Equation (2.5.1).
2.5.1
Vortex Instability
The instability of vortex rings was investigated by Widnall in [56, 57]. She found that
vortex rings are always unstable to the ﬁrst two critical wave modes induced on the
toroidal. Widnall also investigated the instability of helical vortex ﬁlaments, that being
vortex rings and aircraft wake structures, that three modes of instability are present; a
very short-wave mode, a long-wave mode unstable by a local induction and a mutural-
inductance mode. The tiny disturbances on the vortex ring induced by a critical wave
number, form dipoles on the azimute of the vortex ring. As they wrap around the vortex
core, strong streamwise toroidal wave modes are shedding hairpin vortices into the wake
of the vortex ring. New vortex structures eventually lead to turbulence, that destroys the
ring.
The asymptotic analysis was presented to predict induced vortex ﬁlaments of small cross-
sections. As a requirement, the wave length of the perturbations along the vortex ﬁlament,
18
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
should be large compared to the radius of the vortex core. However, the length of the
vortex ring instability would not fulﬁl this requirement. Still, the features of the instabil-
ity, the number of waves and ampliﬁcaton rate showed good agreement between theory
and experiments.
Widnall [54, 55] developed the instability analysis on straight vortex ﬁlaments which are
much less complex before applying the theory to vortex rings, see Figure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: The counter-rotating vortex pairs (left) and the vortex ring (right) exposed to
azimuthal perturbations, by Widnall [56]. a denotes the core diameter, which is equal to 2σ. b
is the distance between the vortex pairs
The instability of vortex pairs to bending wave perturbations, may be investigated from
the velocities, which is a combination of the velocity that stems from the perturbations,
the velocity induced by the displacement of the other ﬁlament of the pair, and the self-
induced circulation of the sinusoidally perturbated vortex ﬁlament. The vortex instability
to a perturbation wave number, kcrit, occur whenever the induced azimuthal velocity at the
ﬁlament by the presence and perturbations of the other ﬁlament, balanced the circulation
of the ﬁlament itself. As a consequence, this yields singularities at the perturbation points,
where the amplitude increases as the ﬁlaments diverges [56]. However, the investiations
were made by the initialization of radial- and centrifugal velocities ﬁelds from a non-zero
circulation, which diﬀer from the setup of the bump vortex structures, presented in this
chapter, with a total circulation of zero. Therefore, the general physical tendencies of
the instability may be compared, such as the divergent points on the vortex ring, but the
critical wave numbers are expected to diﬀer.
2.5.2
Centrifugal Instability Criterions
A possible instability of a vortex ring, may be similar to a Taylor-Coutte ﬂow in which an
instability happens between rotating cylinders, due to unstable arrangement of angular
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
19
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
momentum. The ﬂow between the cylinders, where one is stationary and the other is ro-
tating, becomes centrifugally unstable, as the rotational speed reaches a critical value, in
which toroidal Taylor vortices are induced [43]. In viscous ﬂows, vortex ﬂow regimes are
dependent on the cylindrical Reynolds number and the radii between cylinders. Experi-
ments [2] have hence shown states that include Taylor vortices, wavy vortices, modulated
wavy vortices, vortices with wavy outﬂow boundaries, vortices with wavy inﬂow bound-
aries, vortices with ﬂat boundaries and internal waves, laminar spirals, interpenetrating
spirals, waves on interpenetrating spirals, spiral turbulence, intermittent spots, turbulent
Taylor vortices, turbulence with no large-scale features etc. The concept of the Taylor-
Couette instability is seen in Figure 2.11.
Figure 2.11: Concept of the Taylor-Couette instability in cylindrical coordinates [1]
Due to the vortical motion, the azimuthal momentum is transported radially and the axial
exchange of ﬂuids in vortices transports the azimuthal momentum axially. This tendency
increases for increasing Reynolds number [1]. The azimuthal velocity in cylindrical coor-
dinates shows that the waviness is related to a jet-like azimuthal velocity proﬁle rather
than a radial outﬂow jet. Hence for increasing Reynolds numbers, the axial vortex motion
decreases. The exchange of ﬂuid between neighbouring vortices happen in a cyclic fash-
ion, resulting in that one vortex increases in size while the two adjent vortices decrease
in size. Due to the azimuthal of the background ﬂow, ﬂuid will wrap around the induced
vortices, that correspond the the axial deformantion of the wavy vortex tube [53]. In
generel, these wrap around eﬀects and growing dipoles of vortices are comparable to the
instability regimes investigated on a vortex ring.
For axisymmetric ﬂows, the instability occur as axisymmetric disturbances. Lord Raleigh
investigated the inviscid source of instability as the angular-directed velocity, uϕ, in the

circulation at the inner before the interchange, Γ1. And therefore the circulation at the

Γ2.

equations in polar coordinates, and decomposed the variables into a background state
plus a perturbation as u = u + U. The background state used, is for a Taylor-Couette
ﬂow with inner and outer angular velocities Ω1, Ω2, and radii R1, R2, respectively. By
deﬁnition, ω ≡2Ω, the vorticity and the angular velocity have the same unit [1/s], which
gives reason to believe in similar behavious for vortex rings. By decomposing the Navier-
Stokes equations and removing the background state, Taylor derived the normal mode
perturbation equations [33] as

Figure 2.12: Instability regimes in a Taylor-Couette ﬂow for increasing Ta. Regimes go from

Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations

CHAPTER 2. VORTICITY AND VORTEX DYNAMICS
As seen, the Taylor-Couette ﬂow is unstable, which was ﬁrst based from Raleigh and later
by Taylor. At the critical Taylor number, the onset to instability induces wave modes as
periodic rolls, that do not vary with the azimuthal. By increasing the Taylor number, the
wave modes vary spatially on the azimuthal as well. The wave length of the wave mode
depends on the Taylor number, i.e. the radii. When increasing the Taylor number, the
ﬂow regime eventually becomes fully turbulent.
From the experiments, Taylor found that the critical wave number at the instability onset
was kcrit = 3.12 which corresponds to a critical wave length of λcrit = 2πd/kcrit ≈2d. The
agreement between the experiments and the perturbation equations by Taylor has been
a prime veriﬁcation of the governing Navier-Stokes equations [2]. The Taylor-Couette
instability may be seen as counter-rotating vortices on the toroidal, which is known as
Taylor vortices [2, 43]. The streamlines in the ﬂow include helical ﬂows, in which the
vortices wrap around the center, which induce new vortices. The rates of these are in-
creasing for increasing Taylor numbers. The higher order wave modes eventually leads to
the turbulent regime.
Recktenwald [41] found by experiments, that varying the cylindrical Reynolds number
from 68.2 to 260.9 only varied the critical wave number from kcrit = 3.16 to kcrit = 3.13,
which give reason to believe that the inviscid approach may be reasonable for simulating
the ﬂow. Further, as seen from the results from Widnall [54, 55, 56], the vortex regimes
in the Taylor-Couette instability show similar behaviour compared to the instability in
vortex rings and ﬁlaments. Also, both the vortex ring- and pairs are centrifugal ﬂows,
and it makes sense to compare with known centrifugal ﬂows, such as the Taylor-Couette
ﬂow. As the critical wave length for the Taylor-Couette ﬂow becomes λcrit ≈2d, this may
be translated to the vortex ring as λcrit ≈4σ, where σ in this chapter is deﬁned as the
vortex core radius.
The Raleigh criterion is used for investigations of the bump vortex instability in Chap-
ter 5 on a simple vortex roller, to ﬁgure if the initial setup is stable or unstable. As
the simulations are primarely inviscid, investigations with the Taylor number would yield
Ta →∞. Therefore, as the critical wave number is almost independent on the Reynolds
number [41], the critical wave numbers and wave lengths from the simulations are to be
compared with the ones from the Taylor-Couette theory in Chapter 5 and 6. The latter
instability forms are possible instability forms to be compared with the vortex ring insta-
bility form. However, multiple other instabilities are known for centrifugal instabilities,
which stems from much more complex ﬂows, where the simple assumptions for the above
Taylor-Couette instability are not valid. These instabilities and other hydrodynamic in-
stability forms, which are not centrifugal will be perspectives and discussed in Chapter 7,
as well.
2.6
Unbounded Flow Diagnostics
For the investigation of the ﬂow development, some ﬂow diagnostics relevant for the
unbounded ﬂow are presented, based on the velocity- and the vorticity ﬁelds calculated.
These are used for the analysis of the accuracy of the simulations as well as indicators of

2.6.1
Conserved Quantities
Some quantities should be conserved for the ﬂow development, if the ﬂow is inviscid and
no energy input is present. The invariants of these ﬂow characteristics are deﬁned in a
closed volume, V, where the integral through this volume denotes the conserved quantity.
The ﬁrst quantity has already been deﬁned as the circulation, which was computeted
from the velocity ﬁeld over a closed curve, C, or by using Stokes theorem as the the ﬂux
through a surface, A. Recall

where (b)∗is the complex conjugate. In 3D, the modal energy and enstrophy are calculated
in spectral space using polar coordinates, and in pseudo 3D (2D vorticity patch) the cal-
culation is done in cartesian coordinates with wave number, kz, in the periodic direction.
The structure and dimensions of the vortex ring, may also be calculated from the vorticity
ﬁeld. That is, the centroid velocity, ring radius and core radius. The centroid of the vortex
ring is calculated from the ﬁrst moment of enstrophy, ω · ω, as
XE = 1
E
Z
V
x (ω · ω) dV
(2.6.12)
which is normalized with the enstrophy. The centroid measures the position of the center
of the vortex ring in the propagational axis, x. The vortex ring radius is, similar to the
centroid, calculated from the ﬁrst moment of enstrophy as
RE = 1
E
Z
V
r (ω · ω) dV
(2.6.13)
which is also normalized by the enstrophy. The only diﬀerence from the centroid is the
integration over of radial axis, instead of the propagational axis. For Gaussian vortex
rings, in the laminar regime, the ring is axisymmetric, and the core radius may be ex-
tracted from the ﬁrst and second radial moments of the azimuthal vorticity, which are
normalized by the circulation. The ﬁrst radial moment may be interpreted as the radial
average weight of the azimuthal vorticity, and the core radius, σ, is its spread. The present
procedure was used by Archer, Thomas and Coleman [3] for estimation of the core radius
of a Gaussian vortex ring as the assumption of an axisymmetric core in the laminar stage
was reasonable. However, as the bump vortex ring has a integrated circulation equal to
zero, this procedure may not not be used, and instead the vortex core radius is measured
at the spatial point through core, in which the magnitude of vorticity equals |ω|= 10−5.

3
Numerical Method
Vortex Methods are characterised by the use of the vorticity-velocity form of the Navier-
Stokes equations and by the Lagrangian discretization of the vorticity. The Lagrangian
form of the vorticity-velocity form of the Navier-Stokes equations was derived in Chapter
2. Advantages in using the vorticity variables instead of the Eulerian velocity-pressure
form, are that the pressure term must be solved seperately by an elliptic function, and the
convection term has severe time step limits for stability. The explicit discretization of the
convective term of the Navier-Stokes equations and the associated stabibility constraints,
are avoided by the use of the Lagrangian formulation [18]. The stability criterions for
the methods are described in this section. Vortex methods have, as mentioned, removed
the pressure and does naturally satisfy the continuity criterion, which simpliﬁes the the
numerical methods for solving the equations. If the viscous diﬀusion is low, the volume
of ﬂuid, with signiﬁcant magnitude of vorticity, is a small fraction os the total ﬂuid vol-
ume, which results in a more compact formulation with vorticity than by the velocity [48].
Conventional vortex methods are purely Lagrangian, as they only use discrete particle
formulatins, where the ﬂow is governed by the particle-particle interaction. This however,
shows to be computationally costly for large number of particles, as the N-body problem
scales O(N 2). Furthermore, the method becomes inaccurate when the particles become
odd located and the convervation laws are to some extend avoided [13].
The problems are solved, by introducing a hybrid particle-mesh vortex method, where the
discrete particles, for every time step, is interpolated onto a Cartesian mesh, M, where
the Poisson equations (2.2.3) and (2.2.4) are solved, and then interpolated back to the
advection of particles for the next time step [14, 15, 17]. The hybrid particle-mesh vortex
method scales O(N log N) with the N-body problem, which speeds up the calculation
process. Re-meshing for every time step, the consistency and accuracy are ensured for
this method.
This chapter introduces the discretization and steps of the hybrid particle-mesh vortex
method. The interpolation kernel used for the particle-mesh mesh-particle interpolation
is presented and discussed. A high order regularisation method for solving the Poisson
equations with Gaussian smoothing is brieﬂy described, and so is a method to solve
the Poisson equation in a semi periodic-unbounded domain. Finite diﬀerence schemes of
varying order, are derived for the diﬀerential operators of the discrete form of the vorticity.
A method for re-projecting the vorticity ﬁeld is also presented for the ensurance of the
divergence free vorticity formulation, which was seen in Equation (2.1.5).
27
==PAGE:44==

Hybrid Vortex-In-Cell Method
The hybrid particle-mesh vortex method is called the Vortex-In-Cell method and will for
the remainer be referred to as VIC. Particles are initialized with particle strength, Γp,
which by the VIC are interpolated to underlying, uniform Cartesian mesh, to compute
the mesh vector potential and further the velocity ﬁeld. The eﬃcient method uses vortex
particles which are deﬁned from the the continious vorticity ﬁeld by the convolution

3.1.1
Interpolation Kernel
The convergence and accuracy in conventional vortex methods are weakened due to in-
homogeneous particle distribution. The adaptivity is ensured by remeshing after each
time step of the simulation. The particles are distributed onto the uniform Cartesian
mesh, M, using an interpolation kernel, where the Poisson equations are solved on the
grid nodes of mesh. The order of the interpolation kernel determines the accuracy of the
interpolation between the particle and the Cartesian mesh. Once the velocity ﬁeld and
the vorticity ﬁeld are calculated in Fourier space and re-deﬁned in physical quantities, the
particle derivatives are remeshed from the mesh by the interpolation. The particle-mesh
interpolation is given as

3.1.3
High Order Regularisation with Gaussian Smoothing
The analytical continous Green’s functions are singular at the origin’s, which make them
hard to calculate numerically [29]. Therefore, smoothing regularisation is implemtented,
to introduce a smooth discrete formulation of the Green’s functions to synthesize a con-
tinous solution to the Poisson equations. The contionous Green’s function integral con-
volution is given as
∇2G(x) = −δ (x −x′)
(3.1.24)
where the smooth form of the impulse response equation is given as
∇2G(x −x′) = −ζ (x −x′)
(3.1.25)
where the analogy between the Dirac’s delta function and it’s smooth form is −δ (x −x′) =
−ζ (x −x′). The smoothened form of the discrete vorticity ﬁeld is given by the convolu-
tion
ωϵ = ζϵ ◦ω
(3.1.26)
where ζϵ is a radially-symmetric ﬁlter function with a regularisation radius ϵ [25]. In order
to ensure the conservation of the simulation, the zeroth moment of the ﬁlter function is
deﬁned as
Z
Rj ζϵ(x)dx = 1
(3.1.27)
where j is the number of spatial dimensions. The smooth discrete integration kernel, Gϵ,
for solving the Poisson equation (2.2.3) is hence deﬁned as
∇2Gϵ(x −x′) = −ζϵ (x −x′)
(3.1.28)
The smooth discrete vector potential, deﬁned by the discrete convolution in Equation
(3.1.19) may then be calculated by convolutions as
ψϵ = G ◦(ζϵ ◦ω) = (G ◦ζϵ) ◦ω
⇒
ψϵ = Gϵ ◦ω
(3.1.29)
The smoothing function is a function of the nondimensional radius
ϱ = r
ϵ
(3.1.30)
and the regularisation is done with Gaussian smoothing from the smoothing function
ζϵ(r) = 1

The Poisson equation is solved in Fourier space using Fast Fourier Transforms (FFT) by
zero padding the domain, i.e. doubling the spatial ghost cells with zeros to avoid periodic
images. Next the equations are solved in their spectral wave number space. The spectral
domain is bounded by k ∈[−ks/2, .., ks/2], where ks is the sampling wave number corre-
sponding to the Nyqvist condition.
3.1.4
Semi Periodic-Unbounded Solver
For the simulation of rotating vortex rollers, the Helmholtz theorem is only satisﬁed if
the domain is extended to ±∞, i.e.
periodic boundary conditions.
In this case, the

and periodic directions [12]. In this project, the periodicity is in the z-direction, which
yields a Helmholtz equation
∇2

(3.1.32)

regularised Green’s function in spectral space with two unbounded xy-directions, and one
periodic z-direction is given by its radial inverse Fourier transform in Appendix B.
3.1.5
Finite Diﬀerence Schemes
The diﬀerential operators, i.e. the nabla operator, ∇, and the laplace operator, ∇2, for
the calculation of the particle derivatives in Equation (3.1.5), are approximated using
Finite Diﬀerence schemes of varying order. The schemes are constructed as Central Dif-
ference schemes (CDS), which use the ith stencil as the center point and then use evenly
distributed stencils around the center stencil to deﬁne the order. The number of stencils
needed to achieve the order is q = a + b + 1, where a is the Upwind stencils and b is
the the Downwind stencils [5]. In the present pmlib, the O(h2) and O(h4) schemes are
implemented. Higher order schemes i.e. O(h6) and O(h8) are used in [39]. These schemes
are to be implemented in the present pmlib. The derivation is done following the method-
ologies in [5, 22]. The Finite Diﬀerence coeﬃcient associated to the ith stencil for the ﬁrst
derivative is by deﬁnition zero, and therefore not shown. The schemes are isotropic in all
directions, and are given by some arbitrary function f. The topology of ghost cells has
been extended to include enough mesh cells for the O(h6) and O(h8) schemes. The Finite
Diﬀerence schmes of varying order may be seen in Appendix B.
3.1.6
Re-Projecting the Vorticity Field
As has already been deﬁned in Chapter 2, the divergence of the expression of vortic-
ity, Equation (2.1.4), yielded the divergence free expression in Equation (2.1.5).
The
solenoidal vector potential for describing the velocity was deﬁned in Equation (2.2.1),
where adding a scalar potential, φ, yields the Helmholtz decomposition. Equation (2.1.5)
and (2.2.1) are for convinience repeated as
∇· ω = ∇· (∇× u) ,
u = ∇× ψ + ∇φ
The decomposition replaces the velocity ﬁeld with the vector potential vector and the
scalar potential [27]. For this, the divergence of the vector potential is zero. Due to
the ﬂow is rotational and divergence free, the scalar potential is neglected as ∇φ = 0.
Therefore the Helmholtz decomposition in Equation (2.2.1), combined with the expression
of the vorticity in Equation (2.1.4), yields the Poisson equation (2.2.3). The divergence
of the vorticity may hence be deﬁned as function of the solenoidal vector potential as
∇· ω = −∇2 (∇· ψ)
(3.1.35)

As mentioned, this will in theory be zero, due to the deﬁnation of the divergence free vector
potential . However, the particle trajectories, which are governed by the vector potential,
are solved numerically, where a ﬁnite order of accuracy must be deﬁned. This causes
numerical errors in the particle-mesh interpolation, the solving of the Poisson equation,
the Finite Diﬀerence etc. As a consequence, these errors will produce a vorticity ﬁeld, eω,
that is not entirely divergence free [27, 40]. Therefore the vorticity ﬁeld must be corrected
as

ϑ = ∇· eω = −∇2 

4
Numerical Inﬂuence on the
Vortex Ring Instability
In this chapter, the inﬂuence of the numerical setup, on the vortex ring instability, is
invested. The aim is to ﬁgure out whether the onset to instability can be moved in time,
or whether the lifetime of the vortex ring will avoid the instability, due to the numerical
setup. If the vortex ring instability is indeed independent of the numerical setup, it is
also of interest to ﬁnd the most accurate numerical setup within a computational limit,
to further investigate the physical instabilities.
It should be noted, that the aim of this chapter is not to identify which physical stability,
that may be present. This is discussed in the remaining chapters.
The most important conclusions from the earlier work in [6] are repeated with respect to
the arbitrary c-bump parameter, and it’s inﬂuence of the initial setup and onset to the
instability. The spatial resolution are to be increased to 512 cells over one dimension of
the initial mesh. The eﬀects of increasing the spatial resolution are invested with respect
to the adaptive time step and the accuracy of the simulation. The higher order Finite Dif-
ference schemes, O(h6) and O(h8) to calculate the particle derivatives are tested both on
an inviscid and a slightly viscous, Re = 1 · 107, ﬂow. Hejlesen [24, 25, 26] investigated the
regularisation kernels to solve the Poisson equation, and an arbitrary order regularisation
could be reached. Therefore, the inﬂuence of the regularisation order is not investigated
further.
Varying diagnostics from Chapter 2 are used to investigate the numerical setup. These are
also used for investigation of the onset to instabilities which are compared to isocontours
of the vorticity at the instability onset time step.
4.1
Numerical Setup
The simulations are set up in a Cartesian domain with spatial dimensions x = [−0.5; 0.5],
y = [−1.0; 1.0] and z = [−1.0; 1.0], where the mesh cell size is governed by the spatial
resolution. For the vortex ring simulation, the domain is unbounded. The initial time step
size is dt = 0.01, the CFL number is 0.5 and the Fourier number is 0.5. It has been proved
by multiple authors, that the O(h4) M ∗
6 interpolation kernel increases the convergence
rate and the accuracy of the particle-mesh mesh-particle interpolation compared to the
O(h2) M2 Vortex-In-Cloud and the O(h3) M
′
4 kernels, and therefore only the M ∗
6 kernel
is used for the particle-mesh mesh-particle interpolation. Re-meshing is done for each
37
==PAGE:54==
CHAPTER 4. NUMERICAL INFLUENCE ON THE VORTEX RING INSTABILITY
time step for the ensurence of the convergence in the simulation with a tolerence of 10−5,
and re-patching is done for every 10th time step with a tolerence of 10−6. A maximum
tolerence of the divergence in the simulation is set to 0.01. The regularisation radius is
ﬁxed at ϵ = 1.5. As mentioned the order of regularisation is not changed, but ﬁxed at
O(h10). The time integration for each time step is calculated using a O(dt3) Runge-Kutta
integration scheme. The simulations are primarely inviscid with the exception of the test
of the Finite Diﬀerence schemes, where the Reynolds number is varied from Re = ∞to
Re = 1 · 107.
4.1.1
Initial Core Distribution of Vorticity
The initial core distribution of vorticity is governed by the arbitrary c-bump parameter.
From Equation (2.4.4), it can be seen, that by increasing c, the kurtosis of the bump
stream function is also increasing. As a result, most of the vorticity is distributed in the
center of the vortex core, and the vorticity is rapidly decreasing radially from the vortex
core center. Therefore, by increasing the c value, the vortex core radius is decreasing.
A convergence study of the initial vortex core radius, normalised with the vortex ring
diameter, is seen in Figure 4.1.
0
100
200
300
400
0
0.05

The value of c is varied as 10, 100, 200, 300 and 400. It is seen that the vortex core radius
is converging towards approximately σ = 0.05. By increasing c, and hence decreasing the
vortex core radius, the computational cost is also reduced as far less vortex particles are
needed for the simulation. Also, for low values of c, the vortex ring tends to shed laminar
vortex ﬁlaments in the wake, even before becoming unstable. By increasing the c value,
the size of the shedded ﬁlaments becomes much smaller, and also the time of shedding is
moved to a later time. The aim is to keep the vortex ring smooth and intact until the

instability onset, in which azimuthal waves develop along the toroidal axis of the ring.

laminar vortex ﬁlaments in the wake are also seen.

zero circulation, and the non-zero circulation may be caused by numerical eﬀects. The

is not similar. Therefore the time of the laminar sheddings in the wake are diﬀerent.
However, it is clearly seen that large laminar vortex ﬁlaments are seen in the wake of the
c = 10 ring, where almost none is seen for the c = 400 ring. At the next time step for the
c = 400 ring, which can be seen in Figure 4.3, the instability onset is present, meaning
that the laminar shedding, which can barely be seen in time step 275, may inﬂuence the
onset to the instability. This is investigated further in Chapter 6. It can also be seen that
the vortex ring has propagated longer in space for the c = 10 as the velocity induction is
inﬂuenced by the vortex core.
For the c = 10 ring, the azimuthal magnitude of vorticity is not symmetric. This is not
clearly seen in the initial time step, and therefore must be caused to the azimuthal ve-
locities and the propagation of the ring. For Gaussian initial distributions, only vortex
rings of inﬁnite thin cores are solutions to the Navier Stokes equations [3, 4, 45]. For
simulations with Gaussian initial distribution of vorticity, the transition from the sym-
metric Gaussian distribution to the non-Gaussian equilibrium state, causes the sheddings
of laminar vortex ﬁlaments in the wake. Hence the behaviour in the early stages of the
bump vortex ring, tends to be similar to the Gaussian initial distributed vortex rings.
Therefore, the shedding point can be used as a starting point for which it is believed, that
the vortex ring has reached an equilibrium state. The asymmetry seen for the c = 10 vor-
tex ring, may also be seen for the c = 400 vortex ring, but the asymmetry is much smaller,
as this initial distribution of vorticity is closer to a solution to the Navier Stokes equations.
The onset to the instability, seen in the zy-plane is seen for the c = 400 vortex ring
in Figure 4.3a. In Figure 4.3b the associated energy spectrum among wave numbers is
plotted for the time t∗≈20, in which the instability occurs.

and c = 300, much smaller sheddings are seen in the instability time t∗≈30 and t∗≈24,

the vortex ring with c = 400 sheds a very small vortex ﬁlament and the instability onset
happens around the same time step, this ring proves to be more stable, visually.

associated, compared to the other modes.
This is also seen on the isosurfaces of the
vortex ring, as the instability occur in four nodes around the toroidal axis. If the vortex
ring is simulated later in time, new modes, dividable by 4, will continue to occur on the
toroidal axis, and the circular stage will converge towards a squared stage [6]. At wave
number k = 4, the energy is increased, and the associated wave length may be calcu-
lated as λ = 2π
4 = π
2, which may also be seen in Figure 4.3a. The reason for the critical
wave numbers being distributed in modes dividable by 4, may be due to the Cartesian
mesh-grid setup. It is seen to be independent of the numerical setup for vortex rings
in this thesis. Also, the modes associated to high wave numbers k = 176 and k = 228
have the most amount of energy. For typical wave instabilities [1, 9, 10, 16, 33], the wave
modes associated to low wave numbers have the most energy. Hence, this give indications,
that the wave numbers of the smallest numerical perturbation scales tend to be signiﬁcant.

The ring radius is at the initial stage equal to the setup of RE/(2R0) ≈R0 for the
c = 100, 200, 300, 400 vortex rings. The initial radius for the c = 10 vortex ring is ≈0.52.
The reason for this, is the asymmetry of the vortex core, which is a consequence moves
the core center radially from the centroid position in the vortex ring. The behaviour of
all rings are similar, as the vortex ring radius is immediately increasing to a maximum
point, and then decreases in time. The reason for the decrease in the late stages is that
new wavy modes occur, that extracts energy from the initial ring. The decrease in the
early stages is caused by the non-equilibrium stage.
As seen from Equation (2.4.14), the centroid velocity is initially zero for all rings, which is
indeed true for the simulated cases, as the centroid position in the x-direction is also zero.
The centroid position changes rapidly for the c = 10 as the large asymmetry of vorticity
in the vortex core induces a larger velocity gradient. The centroid position is close to zero
for the higher values of c in the early stages. The rate of change in the centroid position
increases towards the onset to instability. The centroid position in time, i.e. the centroid
velocity is decreasing for increasing c, which again indicates, that high valued c-rings are
closer to a solution to the Navier-Stokes equations, because the circulation is approaching
zero. The vortex ring radius and the centroid position is calculated from the enstrophy,
which relates to the eﬀective viscosity, which may be seen in Figure 4.6 for varying values
of c.

The kinetic energy (not shown) is conerserved in the early stages for all setups, and the
initial kinetic energy is converging towards the kinetic energy associated to the c = 400
vortex ring. As the vortex core radius in much larger for the c = 10 vortex ring, the
kinetic energy is higher. The kinetic energy decreases at a small rate in the early stages,
as the enstrophy is increasing due to instability. The behaviour of the enstrophy is clearly
close connected to the vortex ring radius as the vorticity is distributed in the radial axis.
Even though the centroid position is calculated much similar to the vortex ring radius, it
diﬀers in the sense that the vorticity is zero in the propagational axis, and therefore the
behaviour with respect to the enstrophy is not trivial.
As the simulations are inviscid, the eﬀective viscosity is purely caused by numerical diﬀu-
sion and is in good relation to the enstrophy, as the dissipation only occur due to numerical
eﬀects. As very large numerical diﬀusion is seen in the late stages, the eﬀective viscosity
is only shown from νE = O(10−9), where the simulation is stable for positive eﬀective
viscosity. The results are only of interest until the point in which the numerical diﬀusion
becomes negative.
The eﬀective viscosity for the c = 10 ring is not shown as it provides very large numer-
ical diﬀusion. At the time t∗≈4 the simulation is instantaniously unstable, which was
also seen from the divergence of vorticity (not shown). However, the simulation is stable
thereafter. The instability limits spotted on the isosurfaces for the diﬀerent vortex rings,
correspond well to the numerical diﬀusion. The large numerical instabilities seen at the
times in which the vortex ring instability occur, are due to the three-dimensional eﬀects
of the governing equations become signiﬁcant, and many new small computational scales
become present.
It is once again seen, that the accuracy is increasing for increasing c, which also makes
the instability occur earlier in time, which reduces the simulation time. The circulation
is seen for varying c in Figure 4.7.

For the bump vortex ring, the initial distribution of vorticity ensures the value of zero
circulation. As the simulation approaches the onset to instability, the circulation becomes
negative before rapid ﬂuctuations at the instability time step. The c = 10 vortex ring
is negative already after one time step, where the circulation is zero until the instability
onset for high values of c. The time in which the circulation becomes non-zero is where
the laminar sheddings occur, and the ring starts to propagate. The magnitude of the
circulation governs the size of the laminar sheddings. This is in very good agreement with
the visualizations of the vortex rings in Figure 4.2 and Figures C.3, C.4, C.5 in Appendix
C.
4.1.2
Spatial Resolution
The spatial resolution should be high enough to capture all modes that are present in the
transition from the initial to the instability stage. The spatial resolution was increased
from h/L = 256 in [6] to h/L = 512 for the c-bump validations in this project. Here h
is the uniform grid spacing for one Cartesian cell and L is the domain length. A higher
spatial resolution will increase the accuracy, but also increase the computational cost,
as the time step will decrease and more particles are needed. The spatial resolution is
investigated from a very coarse mesh resolution of h/L = 32 and doubling the resolution
up to h/L = 512. If h/L = 1024, the computational cost exceeds the assigned 96 CPU
cores, and is therefore not investated. The amount of vortex particles and the adaptive
time step for varying spatial resolution are seen in Figure B.1 in Appendix B.
As the vortex particles are interpolated from the mesh, the amount of particles are in-
creasing for increasing spatial resolution. The initial number of particles is not exactly
equal to (h/L)3. This may be due do the ﬁnite order of truncation for the mesh-particle
interpolation. At the onset to instability, the amount of vortex particles is increasing due
44
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 4. NUMERICAL INFLUENCE ON THE VORTEX RING INSTABILITY
to the ﬂow becomes more complex, and more particles are needed to capture the wave
modes. The time step is dependent on the mesh resolution to stabilize the simulation.
Due to a coarse spatial resolution, the time step size for h/L = 32 becomes very large. It
is also seen, that the rate of change in the adaptive time step is large for the coarse mesh
resolution compared to the ﬁner meshes, h/L = 256 and h/L = 512. Even though the
amount of particles is much increased from h/L = 256 to h/L = 512, the time step size
is only increased at an O(12%). Thus the computational cost with regard to time, is not
too high. The eﬀective viscosity in Figure 4.8.

higher associated energy. For a coarse mesh, the enstrophy modes are also decreasing as
it does not capture lower enstrophy modes. This is not shown.
4.1.3
Finite Diﬀerence Order for the Particle Derivatives
In the existing pmlib the O(h2) and the O(h4) Finite Diﬀerence schemes are implemented
to calculate the particle derivatives in Equation (2.1.10). By using more stencils, the
accuracy could be increased by the implemented O(h6) and O(h8) schemes. As already
stated, the central diﬀerence schemes require one more stencil than the order of the
scheme, as the implicit point stencil is the actual particle location. In the ﬁrst time steps,
the amount of ghost points downwind to the ith stencil are extended. That means that
the amount of ghost points is extended 3 stencils downwind for the O(h6) scheme and
4 stencils downwind for the O(h8) scheme. In Appendix C, the isosurfaces are seen for
vortex rings simulated with Re = ∞and Re = 1 · 107 using the O(h6) and the O(h8)
schemes. These are shown at the onset to instability, where no signiﬁcant diﬀerence is
seen between the Finite Diﬀerence schemes. The divergence of vorticity and the eﬀective
46

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

gated further, and the O(h6) scheme is used for further investigations.

The diagnostics are shown for the setup of c = 400, spatial resolution of h/L = 512,

the propagational x-direction is not conserved, which is avoided at the ﬁrst time step.

the equlibrium. At the equalibrium state, which for this vortex ring, is at the onset to
instability, the linear impulse rapidly increases. The angular impulse in the x-direction
(not shown) is conserved to machine precision. The linear- and angular impulses in the y-
and z-directions are conserved to machine precision. The early indications of truncation
may be seen as ﬂuctuations with increasing amplitudes. The same behaviour is seen for
the helicity.
4.3
Summary
The vortex rings were simulated using the O(h4) M ∗
6 interpolation kernel in a Cartesian
domain. The c-bump parameter was seen to inﬂuence the distribution of vorticity in the
vortex core, and hence the vortex core radius. The initial core radius was converging
towards σ ≈0.05 for increasing c, where c = 400 was chosen as an optimum vortex ring
setup, based on smallest and latest sheddings of laminar vortex ﬁlaments in the wake.
These sheddings are proved to be caused by the non-equilibrium stages for Gaussian rings,
where similar behavious are seen for a bump ring. The critical wave modes are seen on the
toroidal axis at θ ∈[0, π
2, π, 3π
2 ] which is independent of the numerical setup. The time in
which the instability occurs depends on the number of particles which is governed by the
vortex core size and the mesh resolution. An optimum spatial mesh resolution is seen for
h/L = 512, which is suitable to capture all wave modes. The accuracy could be slightly
improven by applying the O(h6) Finite Diﬀerence schemes. The O(h8) Finite Diﬀerence
scheme was seen to diﬀer much in the diagnostics for the inviscid vortex ring. The error
was seen again when introducing physical viscosity to the simulation, which the scheme
was not able to capture. Hence the O(h6) scheme is chosen for further investigations.
For the numerical setup, only the linear impulse in the propagational x-direction was not
completely conserved, due to the truncation of the initial vortex ring setup, which is not
a true solution to the Navier-Stokes equations. The linear impulses in the yz-directions,
the angular impulses and the helicity were all conserved to machine precision.
The setup for the simulations in this chapter and the investigated wave numbers are
discussed with respect to the physical instability in remaining chapters.

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

5
Simulation of Vortex Roller
Instability at Re = ∞
In this chapter, the instability of a 3D vortex roller, generated from periodic extended 2D
circular vorticity patches, is investigated. The Lagrangian material form of the vorticity
equation was shown in Equation (2.1.10) and is repeated for convenience as
Dω
Dt = (ω · ∇) u + ν∇2ω
For the 2D vorticity patch, extended to a pseudo 3D vortex roller by periodicity in the
z-direction, the vorticity ﬁeld becomes ω = (0, 0, ωz). As a consequence, the vorticity
stretching term, (ω · ∇) u, vanishes, and the rate of change of vorticity thus becomes
Dω
Dt = ν∇2ω
(5.0.1)
This means that the rate of change of vorticity in 2D becomes a pure diﬀusion problem.
For an inviscid ﬂow, the viscous eﬀects are neglected, and the rate of change of vorticity
can only be non-zero due to instabilities or numerical diﬀusion.
The advantage of this vortex structure is that the nonlinear eﬀects may be neglected and
theoretical instability criterions may be derived for the vortex core as seen in Equation
(2.5.5). This is done both for the bump vortex ring with zero initial circulation and for a
Gaussian distributed vortex ring with non-zero initial circulation. The eﬀects of numerical
cut-oﬀfor the re-mesh, re-patching and the divergence threshold is discussed using the
same numerical setup, which was found to be the optimum in chapter 4. The instability
criterions are investigated for varying c-values for the bump vortex roller and for varying
Gaussian standard deviation, σµ, for the Gaussian vortex roller, where the core radius
should be comparable between the two initial distributions. The investigations are done
both as inviscid and viscous simulations with Re = 1 · 107 for both type of vortex rollers.
Due to neglectable 3D eﬀects for this roller, azimuthal and transverse wave modes may
not be generated even if the vortex roller is unstable. Therefore wave perturbations are
added to the background state to initialize Kelvin waves. This is done by adding sinu-
soidal waves associated to random phases with a ﬁnite amplitude along the center line of
the vortex core.
Bump perturbation equations are derived by perturbating the vorticity and the velocity
and adding these to the background state in the vorticity-velocity form of the 3D Navier-
Stokes equations. The bump perturbation equations may then be found by subtract-
ing the background state equations from the latter equations and neglecting nonlinear
51
==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 5. SIMULATION OF VORTEX ROLLER INSTABILITY AT RE = ∞
perturbation terms. The solution form of these perturbation equations is discussed as
exponential-periodic solutions based on the amplitude of the vorticity- and velocity com-
ponents. Simulations are done for the perturbated vortex rollers as inviscid and viscous
using the same c- and σµ-values, respectively, as for the non-perturbated simulations.
Physical instability forms such as the Taylor-Coutte instability and results from Widnall
[54, 56, 57] are investigated.
5.1
Radial Instability Criterions for Bump and Gaus-
sian Initial Distributions
As mentioned, the advantage of simulating the vortex rollers is that the nonlinear eﬀects of
the Navier-Stokes equations may be neglected if no perturbations are added, and therefore
analytical instability criterias may be derived and compared to the simulations. Thus one
may additionally compare these results to physical instabilities.
By using the bump
function, the core distribution of the streamfunction, the vorticity and the azimuthal
velocity were derived in Equations (2.4.17), (2.4.20) and (2.4.21). The normalized core
distributions may be seen in Figure 5.1.

For both setups, the azimuthal velocity is zero at the center of the vortex core, and in-

tion, the standard deviation has signiﬁcance at the vicinity of the core center and further
troughout the vortex core. However at the outer edge, all setups converge towards the
same azimuthal velocity.

is dependent on the spatial variation of the circulation, which is dependent on the az-
imuthal velocity. Therefore, the spatial point in which an instability is present, may be

can be seen, that in theory, the bump vortex roller is stable from the center of the vortex
core thoughout to approximately half of the core radius, where the criterion becomes
negative. This behaviour is independent of the core radius governed by the c-parameter.
For all setups of c, the ﬂow is unstable at approximately half trough the vortex core. The
spatial point in which the ﬂow becomes unstable is close related to the point just after the
maximum azimuthal velocity, where the velocity gradient is large. The point of instability
is also close related to the the point in which the vorticity has the lowest negative value.
Opposite, it may be seen that the Gaussian setup is stable throughout the entire vortex
core.
5.2
Numerical Setup and Instability without Wave
Perturbations
In this section, simulations of the bump- and the Gaussian vortex rollers are performed
and analysed. The numerical setup for the bump vortex roller is ﬁrst ﬁxed toward the
c = 400 vortex roller. The dependency of c on the instability is investigated both for
inviscid simulations and for slightly viscous simulations. Both the initial instability and
the late stage instabilities are investigated. The simulations are ﬁrst performed without
wave perturbations, to investigate whether the present bump vortex instabilities produce

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

transverse waves in the z-direction, which is seen for Taylor-Couette ﬂows [2, 43, 53]. As
mentioned in Chapter 2, the Taylor-Couette instability is deﬁned in a viscous ﬂow, where
the transition to turbulence is initialized at the critical wave number kcrit to a critical wave
length λcrit, which from viscous experiments [50] are found to kcrit ≈π and λcrit ≈2d,
where d is the diﬀerence in radii between cylinders. The critical wave number is almost
unchanged for varying Reynolds numbers, which gives reason to investigate the instability
as inviscid, and comparing to a slightly viscous ﬂow. The enstrophy and the transverse
