The simulated stability criterions show good agreement with the theoretical values, stating
that the bump vortex roller distribution is unstable, which is true for all time frames. The
spatial point in which the instability becomes present, is unchanged at all times, which
is approximately half trough the vortex core radius, which was also seen from Figure
5.7. Therefore, the instability should indeed be a physical behaviour and not numerical.
The cut-oﬀtolerences are changed in the next sections, to show the independence of the
numerical setup.
5.2.1
Cut-OﬀTolerance for Re-mesh, Re-patch and Divergence
As mentioned in Chapter 3, the hybrid Vortex-In-Cell algorithm is set to re-mesh after
each time step after the particle-mesh mesh-particle interpolation to ensure the conver-
gence of the simulation and to avoid odd displacement of particles. The simulation also
uses re-patching for every 10th time step to ensure the adaptivity of the time step. Last,
in reality, the ﬂow is divergence free, when using the Lagrangian frame of reference. How-
ever, a ﬁnite truncation of the divergence needs to be deﬁned when solving the continuous
ﬁeld as discrete vortex particles, which causes errors and hence divergence of the ﬂow.
So far, the cut-oﬀtolerence has been set to 10−5 for the re-meshing has 10−6 for the
re-patching. The threshold of the divergence in the ﬂow has been set to 0.01. This setup
may be the target for varying the tolerences. The tolerences has been changed to 10−9
for both re-meshing and re-patching and the divergence threshold has been changed to
10−5. The maximum vorticity and the numerical diﬀusion using the new tolerences may
be seen in Figure 5.11.

increased rate of change in the curvature, and hence increased gradients of vorticity, which
are hard to capture for the simulation on the given resolution. The spatial distribution

tional to the azimuthal velocity as
Γ = 2πruϕ
where the maximum value of the circulation is shifted in space compared to the velocity,
due to the proportionality to the radii, as well. Hence the maximum value of the circula-
tion increases for increasing core radius. However, for the bump function, the circulation
is opposite signed at −r, and yields zero at the outer edge of the vortex core, meaning that
the integrated circulation becomes zero and the vortex roller is ﬁxed and non-propagating
in space.
The Raleigh instability criterion for varying c-values for inviscid and viscous simulations
are shown in Figure 5.17.

modes associated to wave numbers diviable to 4 have increased energy. That being wave
numbers k = 4, 8, 12, ..., 512, as the spatial resolution is h/L = 512. Wave modes are thus

low and the modes are not clearly visuable on the isocontour seen in Appendix C. The
lowest wave number associated to high energy, i.e. k = 4 is equal to a wave length of
λ = 0.5, which again is not clearly visuable. The mode associated to the wave number
k = 124 has the highest amount of energy in the early stages of the three-dimensional
eﬀects, and decreases in the late stages. Hence, the cause of these wave numbers may
be due to the numerical setup as a sensitivity to small scales. This is not investigated
further. The temporal variation of the energy modes, are only shown after the instability
occurs. The 0th mode and the ﬁrst 4 modes are shown, where the mode associated to the
k = 4 has increased energy. At the late stages, the wave modes converge towards the 0th
mode while energy is extracted from the 0th mode which should not be physical as no
physical viscosity is present. The behaviour of the growth rate is similar to Winckelmans
trailing wave vortices [59].
5.2.3
Stability Analysis with Gaussian Initial Distribution
The vortex rollers are also initialized using a Gaussian distribution for the setup of the
vorticity ﬁeld. Opposite to the bump function, the Gaussian is directly deﬁned from the
vorticity, whereas the bump function is governed from the vector potential, which is a
derived part of the vorticity. The Gaussian initial distribution has non-zero circulation
and non-zero azimuthal velocity at the outer edge of the vortex core. As mentioned,
the Gaussian setup has limits at ±∞and is hence unnaturally truncated at 2.1σµ. The
purpose of using the Gaussian is to verify the instability of the bump function, which
yielded unstable Raleigh instabilities both in theory and for the simulated cases. Hence,
it is expected, that the non-perturbated Gaussian vortex rollers should become stable all
the way trough the vortex core, to match the theoretical Raleigh stability criterion. The
maximum vorticity and the kinetic energy are shown in Figure 5.19.

It is again seen, that the Gaussian vortex rollers are stable both at the initial condition
and in the the stage. It may be concluded that for non-perturbated vortex rollers, the
bump setups yield unstable ﬂows while the Gaussian setup yield stable ﬂows. However,
no physical wave modes are present, since the ﬂows are primarely 2D, and the 3D wave
modes occur when the ﬂow diverges. Therefore, perturbations may be necessary to induce
physical wave modes to compare with the Taylor-Couette instability wave numbers.
5.3
Investigation of Instability with Azimuthal Wave
perturbations
To induce transverse wave modes for the psuedo 3D vortex roller, the centerline of the
vortex core is perturbated by introducing white noise by linear Kelvin waves, deﬁned in
Equation (2.4.25). The wave perturbations are given a ﬁnite amplitude of a = 10−7, and
initially 24 random phases are generated to induce the perturbations. The perturbations
should be able to induce three-dimensional eﬀects of the simulation, while still maintain-
ing a numerical stability.
First, bump perturbation equations are derived using the Eulerian form of the vorticity-
velocity form of the Navier-Stokes equations.
This is done both in Cartesian and in
cylindrical coordinates. The theoretical solutions to these equations are assumed to be a
periodicity of the perturbation parameters of the vorticity and the velocity.
The same simulations done for the non-perturbated vortex rollers are done by varying
the c-value for the bump vortex rollers, and varying the standard deviation, σµ, for the
Gaussian vortex rollers. The eﬀects of slightly viscous ﬂows are also considered. The
inﬂuence of the perturbation amplitude and the number of random phases are investigated,
by analysing the critical wave numbers and wave lengths. The Raleigh stability criterion
is used to verify if the simulation is unstable compared to the non-perturbated vortex
rollers.
5.3.1
Derivation of Bump Perturbation Equations
The Raleigh criterion is derived for inviscid ﬂows. Taylor modiﬁed the Raleigh criterion
as viscous, by perturbating the velocity-pressure form of the Navier-Stokes equations in
a Couette system between rotating cylinders. The procedure, described by Kundu in [33]
is used to derive the bump vortex perturbation equations using the Eulerian form of the
vorticity-velocity equations from Equation (2.1.9), which is repeated for convenience as
∂ω
∂t + (u · ∇) ω = (ω · ∇) u + ν∇2ω
The velocity ﬁeld and the vorticity ﬁeld may then be decomposed into the sum of the
background state and a perturbation. The decomposed velocity ﬁeld then becomes u =
u+Υ, where the background state is deﬁned from the bump functions as u = (ux, uy, uz),
and the perturbation ﬁeld becomes Υ = (Υx, Υy, Υz). The decomposed vorticity ﬁeld
becomes ω = ω + Ω, where the background state is deﬁned from the bump functions as
72
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 5. SIMULATION OF VORTEX ROLLER INSTABILITY AT RE = ∞
ω = (ωx, ωy, ωz), and the perturbation ﬁeld becomes Ω= (Ωx, Ωy, Ωz). Thus, 6 unknown
parameters are introduced when perturbating the Navier-Stokes equations. Substituting
the perturbations into the Eulerian vorticity-velocity form of the Navier-Stokes equations
in Equation (2.1.9) yields
∂(ω + Ω)
∂t
+ ((u + Υ) · ∇) (ω + Ω) = ((ω + Ω) · ∇) (u + Υ) + ν∇2 (ω + Ω)
(5.3.1)
Expanding the latter equation, the time derivatives, the nonlinear convection and stretcing
terms and the diﬀusion terms stand alone as
∂ω
∂t + ∂Ω
∂t + (u · ∇) ω + (u · ∇) Ω+ (Υ · ∇) ω + (Υ · ∇) Ω=
(ω · ∇) u + (ω · ∇) Υ + (Ω· ∇) u + (Ω· ∇) Υ + ν∇2ω + ν∇2Ω
(5.3.2)
Subracting the background state in Equation (2.1.9) from Equation (5.3.2) and removing
nonlinear teams, only dependent on the perturbations (Ω· ∇) Υ ≈0 and (Υ · ∇) Ω≈0,
the vector form of the vorticity-velocity perturbation equations becomes
∂Ω
∂t + (u · ∇) Ω+ (Υ · ∇) ω = (ω · ∇) Υ + (Ω· ∇) u + ν∇2Ω
(5.3.3)
Thus, the time derivative and the diﬀusion term are only dependent on the perturbated
vorticity ﬁeld, while the nonlinear convection and stretching terms are dependent on the
background velocity- and vorticity ﬁelds. Equation (5.3.3), is available for 2D and 3D,
meaning that the equations should apply both the vortex roller and the vortex rings.
In this chapter, the perturbation equation of the 2D bump vortex roller is derived both
in Cartesian and cylindrical coordinates. The perturbated bump equations are seen in
Appendix A.
The coeﬃcients in the cylindrical bump vortex perturbation equations depend on the
radial component, r and ϕ, and hence the equations admit solutions, which are exponen-
tially dependent on z and t [33]. Therefore, the solution form is considered as normal
modes of the coeﬃcients as
(Υr, Υϕ, Υz, Ωr, Ωϕ, Ωz) =

bΥr, bΥϕ, bΥz, bΩr, bΩϕ, bΩz

exp (ιkz + ςt)
(5.3.4)
where z →±∞is the required bounded domain, due to the Helmholtz criterion. This is
only valid if the wave numbers, k, are real and not imaginary. Hence, the critical wave
numbers may be found by solving the eigenvalue problem when substituting the solution
form in Equation (5.3.4) into the perturbation Equations (A.0.24), (A.0.25) and (A.0.26).
As mentioned, Taylor found the critical wave number for his perturbation equations in a
Couette ﬂow, by experiments to become kcrit ≈π. Solving the eigenvalue problem of the
bump vortex perturbation equations is considered as future work, and is not continued in
this project.

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

It is clearly seen that by perturbating the centerline of the vortex roller, the velocity in
z-direction and the vorticity in the x-y-directions all become non-zero, and hence three-
dimensional eﬀects are now present. The perturbations also show to eﬀect the components,
which are non-zero for the background state as well.
Widnall [54] investigted the stability of helical vortex ﬁlaments with arbitrary core dis-
tribution of vorticity, and found three modes of instability; a very short-wave instability
which probably exist on all curved ﬁlaments, a long-wave mode which is found to be
unstable by a local induction model and a mutural-inductance mode which appears as
the pitch of the helix decreases, and neighbouring turns of the ﬁlament interact strongly.
For a straight vortex roller, only the long-wave mode should be present. Widnall found,
that by increasing the core radius, i.e. decreasing c for the bump vortex, is reducing the
ampliﬁcation rate of the long-wave instability.

are shown in Figure 5.27.

wave length should be twice the vortex core diameter. Therefore, this gives indications,
that either the Taylor-Couette theory is not to be compared with the instability of the
vortex roller or else, the wave number is initialized in the numerical setup. This is further
investigated using the energy spectrum and the temporal variation of energy modes for
the 4 c-values in Figure 5.31.

The Kolmogorov −5/3 law [49] has been plotted, to verify that a developed state has
been reached for all simulations. It should be noted, that this is not a physical turbulence
spectrum, as no viscous eﬀects are present. Therefore, the low energy associated to high
wave numbers are solely based on noise for length scales down to the cell size. It is clearly
seen, that the wave number associated to the ﬁrst mode of increased energy is identical
for all c-values. This is in very good agreement with the isocontours in Figure 5.30. The
wave number associated to this mode is k = 24, which is comparable with the highest
random phase, given to the perturbation wave number. The next three wave numbers
associated to modes of increased energy may be seen in Table 5.1.

wave length given. From the temporal variation of energy modes, the 0th mode has been
shown as a reference, which is increasing in energy for increasing core radius, due to the
increased wave amplitude. The ﬁrst four modes are almost identical with respect to the
energy, and are signiﬁcantly lower in the early stages, compared to the 24th mode. This
again, is most likely due to the given perturbation wave number. At the late stages, the
energy of the ﬁrst 4 modes are converging towards the 24th mode, as the short-wave modes
are extracting energy of the long waves modes which is also seen by Widnall in [56, 57].
This phenomenon eventually leads to the the extraction of energy of the background state
at the 0th mode, which leads to the breakdown of the vortex structure. The ﬁrst 12 wave
numbers to modes of increased energy are extracted, and the corresponding wave lengths,

obtained wave numbers are not comparable with the critical Taylor-Couette wave number
of kcrit ≈π and the associated wave length of 4σ.

5.3.3

The amplitude of the Kelvin wave, which is used for the perturbation of the centerline, has
to be small enough, to keep the simulation stable for some time, to investigate the induced
wave modes. Also, the amplitude should be small enough, such that no visual impacts
of the perturbation may be clearly seen at the initial condition. For the non-perturbated
rollers, the computational time is very high, because the eﬀects of on the vortex roller
is almost zero. Therefore, the amplitude may neither be too small. The vortex roller
with c = 400 is tested using a perturbation amplitude of a = 10−7 and a = 10−10, where
neither perturbation may be seen on the initial condition. The maximum vorticity, the

The critical wave number is easy to spot for most of the vortex rollers as sinusoidal wave

The critical wave length is
measured visually, to match the calculated wave lengths from the energy spectra. It is seen
that if only 12 and 16 phases are used, the sinusoidal wave modes are dispersing. Especially
for 12 random phases, the modes are almost non-present. This may be caused because
the critical wave length becomes too large an covers too many computional cells, which
may not be resolved properly. Therefore, one may not be able to synthesize the critical
wave number of the Taylor-Couette ﬂow of kcrit ≈π. Another problem in synthesizing the
Taylor-Couette wave number, is that the random phases are given in terms of integers.
Indeed, as the computional domain in the z-direction has a ﬁnite length of Lz = 2 meshed
by a spatial resolution of h/L = 512, the possible critical wave lengths may then be seen

5.3.5
Gaussian Perturbations
It has now been concluded, that the bump vortex rollers are always unstable both for
non-perturbated and perturbated centerlines. Also it is seen, that transverse wave modes
may be induced when perturbating the vortex rollers, but that the critical wave lengths
are initialized from the wave numbers given to the perturbations.
It has earlier been concluded, the the Gaussian vortex rollers are stable through the
entire vortex core when not subjected to wave perturbations. The Gaussian vortex rollers
are also subjected to wave perturbations of the centerline, similar to the bump vortex
rollers. This is done to verify, if the Gaussian initial setup is stable as default, or if an

==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 5. SIMULATION OF VORTEX ROLLER INSTABILITY AT RE = ∞
5.4
Summary
In this chapter, straight vortex rollers given initial bump- and Gaussian distributions, are
investigated. The theoretical vorticity and velocity ﬁelds were derived, which were further
used to derive the theoretical distributions of the circulation and the Raleigh stability cri-
terions. From these it could be seen, that in theory, the bump initial setup is unstable
because of a decrease in spatial circulation through the vortex core. The Gaussian initial
setup was in contrast shown to be stable through the entire vortex core due to the in-
crease of spatial circulation. The same numerical setup was used as for the vortex rings in
Chapter 4, and the tolerences of re-mesh, re-patch and divergence did not show to have
importance.
For the non-perturbated bump vortex roller, 2D instabilities were seen on the isocontours
of the vorticity ﬁeld, which occured approximately half trough the vortex core, which were
seen for all core radii. The calculated Raleigh stability criterions showed very good agree-
ment with the theoretical and the visual instabilities, which were present for all times.
The non-perturbated bump vortex rollers were not able to induce three-dimensional wave
modes until the simulation became unstable, caused by pertubations of the smallest com-
putational scales. The non-perturbated Gaussian vortex rollers showed to be stable for
all times, both visually and by verifying the Raleigh stability criterions, which again was
in good agreement with the theorical criterion.
As the bump vortex roller was unstable, but not able to induce three-dimensional wave
modes, the centerline of the vortex core was given wave perturbations initialized by a ﬁnite
amount of wave numbers from random phases. Theoretical bump perturbation equations
were derived in Cartesian and cylindrical coordinates by decomposing the vorticity- and
the velocity ﬁelds with a known background state and a perturbated state. The equa-
tions were then derived by expanding the Navier-Stokes equations and subtracting the
background state and nonlinear perturbation terms. The solution form was thought to
be periodically and the eigenvalues would be the critical wave numbers of the perturbation.
When simulating the perturbated bump vortex rollers for varying c, wave modes were
clearly seen in the z-direction.
These however, were not in good agreement with the
Taylor-Couette theory as the wave length was non changing for varying core size. Neither
did the wave numbers compare to the critical wave numbers found by Widnall in [56, 57].
By varying the number of random phases, and hence the highest wave number given to
the perturbation, this wave number was seen to be governing for the critical wave number.
Hence length scales may in theory be generated by the given perturbation from the length
of the transverse computional domain down to the cell size.
Last it was seen, that wave modes may be added to the Gaussian vortex rollers by per-
turbating the centerlines.
However the wave modes are of order of the perturbation
amplitude and therefore the Gaussian rollers are not able to induce waves. The Gaussian
vortex rollers were further seen to be stable by verifying the Raleigh stability criterion
when the rollers are perturbated.
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
87
==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 5. SIMULATION OF VORTEX ROLLER INSTABILITY AT RE = ∞

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:105==
6
Investigation of the Instability
of Bump Vortex Rings
So far, it has been concluded in Chapter 4, that the vortex ring has an unsteady region in
the early stage as it has not found an equilibrium to satisfy the governing Navier-Stokes
equations. As a consequence, the vortex ring sheds laminar vortex ﬁlaments into the
wake. Thereafter the vortex ring becomes unstable and develops wave modes that wrap
around the vortex core.
In Chapter 5, it was seen, that bump vortex rollers are always unstable, while Gaussian
vortex rollers are stable. Wave modes were only generated for the bump vortex when the
simulation became numerically unstable. Sinusoidal wave modes could be generated by
perturbating the centerline of the vortex core. However, these wave modes did not match
any theoretical wave number or lengths as the wave modes were primarely dependent on
the perturbation wave number. In this chapter, the instability seen on the vortex ring is
compared to the wave quantities from theory.
6.1

The 3D perturbation equations derived in Chapter 5 are derived as

The full form of the 3D bump vortex ring perturbation equations may be derived by
inserting the theoretical bump vortex ring velocity- and vorticity ﬁelds into the latter
equations which yield highly complex equations which are dependent on x, y and z. The
solution form of the perturbation equations is therefore not as trivial as for the bump
vortex roller, and is not investigated further.

For the c = 100 vortex ring, the core is increasingly dissipated compared to the other

It should be mentioned, that the numerical diﬀusion is increased for this ring, even though
the vorticity gradients are higher for the vortex rings with high c-values.
6.2.2
Investigation of the Critical Wave Number
The critical wave number of the Taylor-Couette instability was found by Taylor to be
approximately kcrit = π.
This was seen to be dependent on the Taylor number, Ta,
relating the centrifugal forces with the viscous forces in a Couette ﬂow. The critical wave
number was seen, though, to be almost independent of the viscosity, meaning that the
inviscid simulations with Ta = ∞should be suitable for comparison. The critical wave
length, estimated from the critical wave number was seen to be twice the diﬀerence in
radii, which may be seen in Figure 6.3.
Figure 6.3: Theoretical concept of the critical wave length of the Taylor-Couette instability [30]
The critical wave in this system, is hence covering two counter-rotating vortices. Widnall
[56, 57] further investigated, that the vortex rings are always unstable to the ﬁrst two
critical wave numbers of κ = 2.5 and κ = 4.45 where κ = kσ. This was seen only to be
true if σ ≪R which is increasingly true for increasing c of the bump vortex ring. The
wave modes present at the instability onset for the varying c-values are seen in Figure 6.4.
92
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 6. INVESTIGATION OF THE INSTABILITY OF BUMP VORTEX
RINGS
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 6.4: The critical wave on the toroidal at the onset to instability at (a) c = 100, (b)
c = 200, (c) c = 300 and (d) c = 400
As mentioned, the reason for the critical waves being initialized on the 4 nodes of the
vortex ring, may be due to numerical eﬀects. One may notice, that multiple small waves
are seen on the toroidal of the vortex rings. However, one may also consider the entire
range of small waves in one node as one wave length. If the toroidal point at θ = π/2 is
considered, the small waves expands in opposite directions of the node to a ﬁnite length,
where no waves a present. This is to some extend a similar behaviour to the Taylor-
Couette theory in Figure 6.3, where two opposite direction vortices induce the critical
wave. Thus, the cricital wave of the vortex ring may be the length of all the waves at
one note. For c = 400, this long wave length is in fact very close to twice the vortex core
diameter, which correspond to the Taylor-Couette theory. However, this is in paradox to
the increasing vortex core radii for the ramainding vortex rings. If the Taylor-Couette
theory should be present, the critical wave lengths on the toroidals should be increasing
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

for increasing core radii. However, as the vortex ring with c = 400 is the most numerical
stable ring, the theory may be in good agreement. If the vortex ring with c = 400 actually
does represent the critical wave length of the Taylor-Couette instability, it may be due to
the small gradient of the curvature of the toroidal. For increasing c, the wave length of
the inner and the outer radii is converging towards the same length, due to the decrease
of the curvature gradient. The critical wave modes to associated wave numbers and the

As mentioned, Widnall [54] investigated three modes of instability in helical vortex ﬁla-
ment ﬂows; a very short-wave instability which probably exists on all curved ﬁlaments,
a long-wave mode found to be unstable by the local-local inducion model and a mutual-
inductance mode which apprears as the pitch of the helix decreases and the neighbouring
turns of the ﬁlament begin to interact strongly. One may argue that the short-wave and
the long-wave modes are present on the vortex ring, if the entire range of small waves of
one node is one wave length. This is diﬃcult to verify, as the cause of the critical waves
being onset on the 4 nodes is still unknown.

6.4
Summary
The wave modes of the instability stage have been discussed and compared to the Taylor-
Couette theory and to the results of Widnall [54, 56, 57].
The 3D bump vortex ring perturbation equations derived in Chapter 5 were presented.
The complexity of the vortex ring ﬂow make the solution form hard to identify.
By increasing the c-value, the vortex core radius is decreasing, which has been seen mul-
tiple times in this project. The non-equilibrium stages have higher imporatance of the
numerical diﬀusion of the low valued c-rings.
At the onset to instability, wave modes are seen on the toroidal at 4 nodes on the vortex
ring. If the length of the opposite directions small wave domains is considered as one
long wave length, this critical wave length correspond well with the critical wave length
of the Taylor-Couette instability for c = 400. As the reason for the critical waves being
located at the 4 nodes is unknown, the match between wave lengths may be a coincidence.
If the nodes in fact consist of multiple small waves and one long wave, this is in good
agreement with the results from Widnall [54], who identiﬁed both short-wave and long-
wave instabilities on curved vortex ﬁlaments.
Last it was seen, that slightly viscous eﬀects do not make an visual impact on the wave
modes at the instability stage.

==PAGE:113==
7
Discussion
It has been seen, that the bump vortex rollers are unstable from the Raleigh criterion.
This was also seen by Raleigh to be the case for the Couette ﬂow, between cylinders
where the inner cylinder is rotating and the outer cylinder is stationary. The cause for
this behaviour is that Γ2 decreases radially. The bump setup of the vorticity, and hence
the velocity, is able to produce such a distribution which initially is unstable. As earlier
stated, if the ﬂow is unstable, the given perturbations to a system is growing, while the
perturbations are reducing and vanishing for a stable system, which was seen for the
Gaussian setup.
However, the Taylor-Couette theory does not match with respect to the critical wave
numbers of the bump vortex rollers. The wave numbers calculated from the given wave
perturbations showed be to dependent on the highest wave number given by the num-
ber of random phases. From the simulations of bump vortex rollers without given wave
perturbations, the wave numbers with highest associated energy are very high, where the
highest wave number is k = 124. This clearly diﬀer from the Taylor-Couette theory where
kcrit ≈π. The behaviour of this, again shows that the highest perturbations given to a
system, whether it is sinusoidal Kelvin waves or numerical perturbations, become the
most signiﬁcant.
The critical wave numbers found by Widnall [56, 57] do not match well with the simu-
lated bump vortex rings. The assymptotic solution found by Widnall, is based on a setup
of a vortex ring, where the circulation is non-zero, which was in good agreement with
results optained by experiments. The bump vortex ring may be non-physical because a
distribution with a total circulation of zero is hypothetical. The distribution of the bump
vortex ring and vortex roller is done with the smooth bump function, which has inﬁnite
number of derivatives. As seen, the sign of the vorticity changes troughout the vortex
core. Such a distribution may not be possible to generate by experiments.
On the other hand, a Gaussian distributed vortex core is seen to be stable throughout
the entire vortex core with respect to the Raleigh criterion, for the vortex roller. This is
further validated, as no wave modes are induced by time, and if wave perturbations are
given, these vanish by time, which is also stated in [9] for a stable ﬂow. A Gaussian distri-
bution has, similar to the setups by Widnall, a non-zero circulation. For vortex rings, the
Gaussian setup becomes unstable by time due to the asymmetry of the core distribution
[3, 4]. This is conﬁrmed by direct numerical simulations (DNS), similar to this project,
and is validated by experiments. However, for the vortex roller, the Gaussian setup does
97
==PAGE:114==
CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
not match well with the Taylor-Couette ﬂow, because the Taylor-Couette ﬂow becomes
unstable in a similar manner to the bump distributed vortex setup. As the cylinders are
ﬁxed in space, this corresponds well with the bump vortex setup with zero circulation.
Referring to Equations (2.5.6) and (2.5.7) the perturbation equations derived by Taylor,
are only dependent on the radial variation and is periodic in the z-direction. This may
give indications, that the Taylor-Couette ﬂow is more suitable for comparison to the bump
vortex rollers, rather than the bump vortex rings.
Further it has been seen, that when three-dimensional eﬀects occur for both the vortex
roller and the vortex ring, the energy spectra have increased energy at every mode di-
vidable by 4, to become an integer number. The cause for this does not seem physical,
but is most likely due to the numerical setup. By varying the arbitrary c-value and hence
the vortex core size does not eﬀect this behaviour. Also, the behaviour is not changed
when varying the spatial resolution, ﬁnite diﬀerence order and the regularisation order
(not shown). The Cartesian mesh for the particle-mesh mesh-particle interpolation may
be the cause. However, a O(h4) interpolation kernel, M ∗
6, is used which has proven to be
much accurate by multiple authors.
As mentioned, the critical wave numbers seen on the spectrum for the vortex ring are
every modes diviable by 4. The energy is not increasing nor decreasing for high and low
wave numbers within these modes divable by 4. Therefore, the critical wave length, which
may be calculated from the spectrum, is hard to identify on the toroidal of the vortex
ring. At the onset to instability, all wave modes are seen on 4 nodes i.e. θ ∈[0, π
2, π, 3π
2 ].
The length of the entire wave domain at such note is approximately twice the vortex
core diameter of the c = 400 vortex, which has been seen to be the most accurate and
numerical stable vortex ring in Chapter 4. This is in principle in good agreement with the
critical wave length from the Taylor-Couette theory, but is not consistant, if the vortex
core diameter is varied. Therefore this may be a coincidence. Also, it is hard to verify, if
this length on the toroidal is an actual wave length, or a numerical behaviour, based on
the hybrid vortex method.
7.1
Centrifugal Instability
Some assumptions are done regarding to investigating the bump vortex instabilities as a
Taylor-Couette instability, which may compromise the actual physics, which is present.
First, the Taylor-Couette instability is dependent on the Taylor number, relating the cen-
trifugal forces to viscous forces. The critical Taylor number which yielded the critical
wave number k ≈π, is dependent solely on the angular velocities on the inner and outer
cylinders in the Couette ﬂow, and hence not directly dependent on the viscosity. This
led to the assumption that inviscid simulations would apply, and the critical limit for the
bump vortex could become dependent on the vorticity destribution. The assumption of
neglectable viscous eﬀects was further done based on the experiments by [41] as the crit-
ical wave number was almost independent on the Reynolds number. The critical Taylor
number is given as function of the inner and outer angular velocities, and is hence always
unstable, which is in good agreement with the bump vortex roller, as mentioned.
98
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:115==
CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
The Taylor number given in Equation (2.5.8), implies that for inviscid simulations, the
Taylor number becomes Ta →∞. For the critical Taylor number in Equation (2.5.9),
this implies that the fraction between outher and inner angular velocities Ω2/Ω1 →−1.
As vorticity, by deﬁnition, is equal to twice the angular velocity, this may be deﬁned as
ω2/ω1 →−1. As the bump vorticity distribution shifts from positive to negative through
the vortex core, the negative sign makes sense. However, the actual magnitude is not
equal to 1, because of the deﬁnitions of the bump vortex radius. For the Taylor-Couette
ﬂow, the negative fraction between the angular velocities may be seen as counter-rotating
cylinders. As the vorticity distribution of the Gaussian setup is positive troughout the
entire vortex core, the critical Taylor number is not possible to deﬁne for this case, and
the ﬂow is stable.
The Taylor number should be reduced to the limits for which experimental results could
be matched. However, reducing the Taylor number of the bump vortices, would mean to
introduce very high kinematic viscosities. For bump vortex rings, reducing the Reynolds
number to Re = 1 · 107, similar results compared to the inviscid simulations are seen, but
decreasing the Reynolds number further, would yield simulations in which no instability
occur. This is also the case for the bump vortex rollers, when reducing the Reynolds
number to Re = 1 · 105 (not shown). This obviously shows a diﬀerence in between the
simulations and the theory from experiments.
The Reynolds number is also diﬃcult to deﬁne for the bump vortices, as the circulation
is zero, meaning that the circulation-based Reynolds number, which is a common choise
for the investment of cylindrical ﬂows, would yield zero, even if it is Reynolds number for
the simulation is deﬁned as inﬁnity. Also, due to the circulation is zero, the propagational
velocity of the vortex ring and roller, also becomes zero.
Experiments have been performed by multiple authors to produce many diﬀerent Couette
ﬂows. A Couette ﬂow is also present if the cylinders are rotating at same angular velocity,
and if the inner cylinder is ﬁxed while the outer is rotating. Also the Couette ﬂow is both
for cylinders rotating in same directions and by counter-rotating cylinders. In general the
Couette ﬂow is quantiﬁed by the relative velocity between the cylinders, which governs the
velocity distribution. The Taylor-Couette instability was deﬁned as the outward centrifu-
gal force is exceeding the inward pressure gradient force. This is seen on the bump vortex
roller where Γ2 is decreasing some point radially, in which the ﬂow becomes unstable.
This correspond to the case of Couette ﬂows in which the inner cylinder is rotating and
the outer is stationary. From the critical Taylor number, all Couette ﬂows become unsta-
ble, and the order of instability perturbations are governed by the fraction between the
angular velocities at inner and outer radii. Finding a bump vortex distribution for describ-
ing other types of Couette ﬂows may not be possible, and hence, the bump vortex roller
instability may only be comparable to one type (if any) of the Taylor-Couette instabilities.
One may also consider whether the Taylor-Couette theory by itself is a unique solution to
the Navier-Stokes equations. The vortices seen in the Taylor-Couette ﬂow are known as
Taylor vortices. Vortices of such, that occur in centrifugal ﬂows are known as secondary
ﬂows, as they are superimposed on the primary ﬂow [33]. However, other types of sec-
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
99
==PAGE:116==
CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
ondary ﬂow in addition to the Taylor vortices are commonly seen in cylindrical ﬂows. In
these types of ﬂows, the curved streamlines and the viscosity, induce centrifugal forces.
The one ﬂow is a pressure gradient driven ﬂow through a curved channel and the other
ﬂow is the so called G¨ortler vortices seen at the boundary layer of a concave wall [33]. As
more than one secondary ﬂow may be present, this shows that the governing Navier-Stokes
equations may be nonunique, as more than one steady solution is present for the same
boundary conditions. Taylor derived the Taylor-Couette theory on the base that only one
secondary ﬂow is present by the assumption that the radial and transverse velocities in
a cylinder is neglectable. This however, showed to be a good assumption as experiments
showed good agreements to his derived perturbation equations.
From the bump perturbation equations derived in this project, it is seen, that an az-
imuthal variation of the perturbated vorticity components,
uϕ
r
∂Ωi
∂ϕ , is present. For the
bump vortex setup to be similar to the Taylor-Couette theory, this component needs to
be very close to zero, otherwise more than one secondary ﬂow may be present for the
bump vortex roller, in which the bump perturbation equations would be a nonunique
solution to the Navier-Stokes equations.
In general, the vortex ring and the Couette ﬂow diﬀer in coordinates. Referring to the
poloidal- and toroidal coordinates in Figure 2.2, the azimuthal for both the Couette ﬂow
and the vortex ring is poloidal. The normal to this direction is the tranverse z-direction
for the cylindrical Couette ﬂow, while the normal is the toroidal axis for the vortex ring.
Therefore, if the vortex ring is to be compared to the Couette ﬂow, the criterion for the
vortex core radius and ring radius, σ/R ≪1 must be fulﬁlled, such that a small toroidal
segment of the vortex ring may be considered as a cylinder. Another complication of the
vortex ring, is that if the velocity distribution of a Couette ﬂow is identical to the vortex
core distribution, it only covers half of a vortex core, see Figure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Rotating frames of the Couette ﬂow and the vortex ring. Azimuthal rotating is
into the plane. Only half the cross-sections are shown
The sketch shows half the radial cross section of the ﬂuid domain in the Coutte ﬂow

and the vortex ring. The dashed lines in the radial axis, r, mark the axis of rotation.
The azimuthals are into the plane.
The cross section of the Couette ﬂow is rotating
around the origin of the two cylinders, where the vortex ring is rotating around its own
imaginary axis, and hence not the centroid of the vortex ring. This again conﬁrms that
the bump vortex roller is more similar to a Couette ﬂow rather than the bump vortex ring.
The vorticity distribution has by numerous authors [21, 31] been seen to have inﬂuence
on the stability. Linear stability are seen for circular vorticity patches with uniformly
distributed vorticity through the vortex core . Further the symmetry of the vortex core
has been seen to have inﬂuence of the stability, where a rotating Kirchhoﬀvortex elliptical
vortex becomes unstable. As the uniformly distributed vorticity vortex core may not
represent a Coeutte type ﬂow, it makes sense that the uniform distribution is stable
while the Coeutte distribution is unstable. For an elliptical distribution, in which radius
varies with the azimuthal, the ﬂow becomes unstable even for Gaussian distributions.
Last barotropic instabilities have also been investigated by numerous authors were small
perturbations on axisymmetric vortices lead to high order multipoles of the 2D vortex
structure [7, 8, 23], which can be compared to the bump vortex.
7.2
Critical Wave Numbers
The instabilities observed for the bump vortex roller were primarely based on the Raleigh
criterion, which is based on the inviscid assumption. Taylor derived the analysis for the
critical wave numbers, in which the instability occur, based on viscous ﬂows. As men-
tioned, it was later seen, that viscous eﬀects had little inﬂuence.
This has also been
veriﬁed in this project. If the Reynolds number is kept below Re = 1 · 103, the critical
wave numbers of the energy spectra still is dependent on the given perturbations.
As it has been noticed from the simulations, the critical wave number of the perturbated
vortex rollers is directly dependent on the given amount of random perturbation phases.
For the case of simulations without given wave perturbations, the critical wave numbers
from the spectrum are seen to be for the high wave numbers, that depends on the spatial
resolution, where the wave number is approximately a fouth fraction of the spatial reso-
lution. Liftschitz [34], also investigated for invisid standing wave instabilities in rotating
ﬂows to be dependent on high wave numbers, where the amplitude increases for decreasing
spatial scales. These instabilities are probably the same as the 2D bump instabilities.
There may be physical ﬂows which are sensitive to high wave numbers regarding to the
instability onset, such as plasma physics and other types of shear ﬂows. However, the
physics of these ﬂows are not well comparable to the cylindrical ﬂow of the vortex ring
and vortex roller, and is hence not investigated. The tendency in which the critical wave
numbers is also seen in a study on a bypass transition [36], where the spanwise oscillations
in a boundary layer at the transition to turbulence, are investigated. In this analysis,
the visualizations showed that the high-amplitude forcing parameters are the result of a
substantial weakening of the low-frequency boundary layer streaks. But at high forcing
amplitudes are associated to new high frequency instabilities in the pre-transitional ﬂow.
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
101
==PAGE:118==
CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
7.3
Further Work
As the critical wave number is hard to qualify in this project, as it for given wave pertur-
bations become equal to the given wave number based on the number of random phases.
On the other hand, the critical wave number seems to be dependent on the smallest com-
putational scales of the mesh if no wave perturbations are given, which may not represent
a physical ﬂow. It could be necessary to introduce a subgrid scale model in form of a
Large-Eddy Simulation (LES) model. The concept of this model is to explicitly solve
for the the large eddies in ﬂow and implicitly model the smallest scales of the ﬂow with
subgrid-scale model. The scales to be solved explicitly for, is spatially averaged with re-
spect to a given minimum size scale. That means that scales below this scale are modeled.
The purpose of using a LES model instead the direct numerical simulations (DNS) in this
project, is to remove the resolved small scales, which governs the critical wave numbers
for the simulations without perturbations. In [35], a Lagrangian LES scheme was seen
to capture experimentally observed results of vortex rings, including the turbulent break-
down on small-scale structures.
Next, in this project, the approach has been entirely based on numerical simulations. This
is a developing tool within science, because physical experiments are expensive, and many
physical aspects, such as turbulence are diﬃcult to produce. Also the computational tools
for simulations are increasingly developing, which removes many limitations for simula-
tions. However, to test the validity of the simulations, targets are needed for comparison.
This is often done from experiments, which has been seen for some vortex rings. The
bump vortex ring is hypothetical, and hence physical experiments may not be possible.
Thus a proper validation tool may be by an analytical mathametical approach, where to
solve the bump perturbation equations, which are derived in this project. As mentioned,
the solution form to this problem is based on the normal modes of the perturbation com-
ponets which is spatially periodic. Hence by solving the eigenvalue problem, one would
get the theoretical critical wave numbers, which can be compared to the observed wave
modes at the instability onset.
Last, it may be necessary to investigate other types of vortex instibilities. In this project,
the primary target has been on the similarities between the bump vortex ﬂows and the
Taylor-Couette ﬂow. The reason for this was that the Taylor-Couette instability occur as a
centrifugal instability, and show many similar behaviours to the bump vortex instabilities.
In aerodynamics, another instability, which is often spotted behind aircraft wakes, is the
Crow instability. In such instability, the tip vortices in the wake interact with the contrails
of the engines. The instability occur as counter-rotating vortices disturbe each other and
induce small sinusoidal disturbances. Hence, these grow in a similar manner as investiaged
by Widnall [54], and form a chain of vortex rings. This instability is probably not present
for the bump case, as no axial velocity in present for the initial setup. Last, one may
also analyse the distribution of the initial vorticity in the vortex core. Multiple authors
[21, 32] investigated the presence of an elliptical distributed vortex core, where the radii
change with the azimuthal. The instablity that occured for these initial Gaussian setups
is much similar the instability seen for the bump vortex roller in Figure 5.7. Elliptical-
and barotropic instabilities should therefore also be considered.
102
Hybrid Particle-Mesh Vortex Method Simulations
of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions
==PAGE:119==
8
Conclusions
Bump vortex instabilities are simulated and investigated in order to qualitatively under-
stand the complex transition from the laminar to turbulent regime in ﬂows. The simu-
lations are done on vortex rings and straight vortex rollers of circular vorticity patches.
The instabilities are seen as small wave disturbances on the systems parameters, that
grows and form new disturbances, that eventually leads to turbulence. The simulations
are primarely run as inviscid, and hence do not represent a physical turbulence ﬁeld.
The vortex ring and vortex roller were initialised with a radial ”bump” distribution of
vorticity through the vortex core. The bump function is a compact, inﬁnite diﬀerentiable,
function within a supported domain and is hence not unnaturally truncated as compared
to a Gaussian distribution. The bump vortex has a total initial circulation of zero, mean-
ing the the vortex is non-propagating. The instability forms seen on the bump vortex
structures show visual similarities to the Taylor-Couette instability, which occur in ﬂuid
domains within rotating cylinders. In such ﬂows, the instability occur due to unstable
arrangement of angular momentum and develop wavy modes which are ﬁrst transverse,
then develop to azimuthal waves and eventually become turbulent. The aim of the anal-
yses, was to investigate the bump vortices as a Taylor-Couette instability.
The vortex rings and vortex rollers were simulated with a hybrid particle-mesh vortex
method using a O(h4) interpolation kernel. A high spatial resolution of 512 cells over
one computional direction was used, together with a O(h10) regularisation kernel for solv-
ing the Poisson equation and a O(h6) ﬁnite diﬀerence scheme for calculating the particle
derivatives.
The arbitrary c-parameter of the bump function was governing the vortex core radius,
and the core was descreasing for increasing c, and further converges towards a solution to
the Navier-Stokes equations. This was seen as minimum amount of laminar sheddings of
ﬁlaments in the wake, caused by non-equilibriums of the setup. This was veriﬁed as the
circulation becomes non-zero. The vortex ring with least sheddings was chosed as c = 400.
This was also chosen for the vortex roller for comparison. For the vortex ring, the critical
wave modes were seen on the toroidal axis at θ ∈[0, π
2, π, 3π
2 ]. Modes associated to wave
numbers diviable by 4, all have signiﬁcant increased amount of energy on the spectra. The
highest energy was associated to high wave numbers, which vary with the mesh resolution.
For c = 400, the toroidal domain of wave modes was approximately twice the length of the
vortex core diameter. This was in principle in agreement with the viscous Taylor-Couette
theory, but was most likely a coincidence, due to inconsistence with varying c-values.
103
==PAGE:120==
CHAPTER 8. CONCLUSIONS
From an inviscid ﬂow, Taylor derived the Raleigh criterion of a Couette ﬂow, which states
that the ﬂow is unstable if the circulation squared decreases radially.
From this, the
mathematical distribution of a bump- and a Gaussian vortex roller yields, that the bump
vortex roller is initially unstable while the Gaussian roller is stable. Simulations of bump-
and Gaussian distributed vortex rollers, without added perturbations, conﬁrmed this the-
ory, and showed to be independent of the core radius and time. The spatial point of the
critical Raleigh criterion in which the ﬂow is 2D unstable showed good agreement with
isocontours, where a noisy region approximately half through the core was spotted. At
the late stages, asymmetric vortex structues were spotted, which are also seen for elliptic
vortex structures, simulated by multiple authors. This behaviour was not investigated
further in this project. At the very late stages the ﬂow induced 3D wave modes, which
had similar behaviour to the vortex ring, where high wave numbers, based on the mesh
resolution, were signiﬁcant.
The bump perturbation equations were derived mathematically in 2D and 3D, by decom-
posing the components of the governing Navier-Stokes equations into a background state
and perturbation. The solution form to the equations is expected to be a periodicity of
the normal modes of the perturbation compenents, where solving the eigenvalue problem
would yield the critical wave numbers. This is considered as further work.
The viscous Taylor-Couette theory states that the onset to instability occur at a critical
wave number of kcrit ≈π. The simulations were kept inviscid and slightly viscous, as
experiments have shown that the critical wave number is almost indepent of the Reynolds
number. To induce Kelvin waves, seen in Taylor-Couette ﬂows, the center line of the
vortex structure was given perturbations based on a ﬁnite number of random phases. For
the bump vortex simulations, this proved to induce transverse wave modes in the periodic
z-direction. The wave modes were initially not seen, but developed by time, and eventu-
ally the vortex broke down, which compared well to ﬂow instability analysis. However,
the critical wave number, showed to be dependent on the highest given perturbation wave
number, dependent on the number of random phases. In theory the wave length may vary
from the transverse domain length down to the mesh cell size. From ﬂow diagnostics, the
perturbations could be seen, initially, for the Gaussian vortex rollers, but then vanished
by time, which was in good agreement with theory of ﬂow stability.
From the inviscid Raleigh criterion, the bump vortex roller is unstable while the Gaus-
sian roller is stable. The instability of the bump vortex roller is comparable with the
Taylor-Couette ﬂow, which is always unstable. The simulated critical wave number is
dependent to the highest given pertubations. For simulations without added perturba-
tions, the system is sensitive to high wave numbers that vary with the mesh resolution. If
perturbations are given, the system is sensitive to the highest perturbation wave number,
given by the number of random phases.
For further investigations, one may need to include a LES model, that model the smallest
scales in the simulations and only solves the largest scales. Also, a mathematical approach
could be necessary, by solving the bump perturbation equations with respect to the wave
number. Last, it may be necessary to investigate for asymmetry and elliptic instabilities.
104

of Bump Vortex Instability in Three Dimensions

The solution form of these Cartesian bump vortex roller perturbation equations is not
trivial, as the unknown depend both of x and y.
As seen from the non-perturbated
equations, the vorticity- and velocity ﬁelds depend on the radial component, r, only, and
therefore the perturbation equations are also to be derived in cylindrical coordinates.
